1.6 Dialectics
         Te Algebra of Revolution
         R D
I oe ro Russia, 1917, an the hettos o Chiao, where I irst saw a blak erson. The reason that I’ startin that way is that I was illiterate. You’re born in a borer town. There’s a revolution, there’s a ounterrevolution, there’s anti-Seitis. You know nothin but exeriene a lot, eseially i you haen to be born a revolutionary. You on’t know that you’re a revolutionary, but you’re oose to everythin.
     I the aitalists were only exloitin us, they wouln’t last a inute.
It’s beause they have all the ass eia, as well as the exloitation, all the euation, everythin with whih to brainwash us an ake us think that their ieas are our ieas—“I I only think about ysel an y aily, I will et soewhere” is that tye o iea—that they are able to eretuate this exloitative syste.
     I you live when an iea is born an a reat revolution in the worl is
born, it oesn’t ake any ierene where you are.  cm   g f  dpm f m. You know it in your bones in soethin as sile as when you say, “No!” to your aa, who wants to ut you in ink an the boy hil in blue.
     Take Rosa Parks. Do you think she thouht she was startin a revolution?
No, she was tire as all et-out! She ha ust worke a ull ay. She was tire an ust wasn’t oin to et u aain to ove to the bak o the bus to ive her seat to a white an who han’t labore as har as she. An the blak youths who were sittin there seein this ile-ae woan bein rae o to the olie station sai, let’s not let Rosa Parks be all alone there.
     What i Rosa Parks o by that one ation? She starte the entire blak
revolution in the South! She’s the one who ae Rev. Martin Luther Kin Jr. the “leaer.” Kin woul never have been leaer i a oveent or reeo han’t starte ro below, sontaneously.
     What is iortant is that you are so natural an oonent o this syste
that you will brin on the revolution. Your one ation o oosition to the syste akes you art o that revolutionary oveent, an you i it, not beause you were “unonsious”—that’s what they think you were—but be- ause you were born a revolutionary an on’t like the an syste uner whih we live!
     Take the question o ale hauvinis. Suenly ust a “ersonal,” “a-
ily” aair akes you rebel. It isn’t that you ae a ateory alle “a ove- ent ro ratie to theory; a oveent ro theory to ratie.” It isn’t in

##  h  / P   I s | 55

any book you rea. The book ay have ae a eneralization about it, but it was in lie, in your lie, an beause it was also in other lives, an they too rebelle, it beae a oveent, an a book, an an oranization.
     One other inient hasn’t to o with woen but with blaks. I was in
Paris in 1947, tryin to onvine Trotskyists they shoul believe that Russia isn’t erely a “eenerate workers’ state,” it’s a state-aitalist soiety. When there are reat stirrins in lie, in revolutions-to-be, soethin ets in the air an rosses national bounaries.
     The oint is: it oesn’t ake any ierene whether there was an in-
erson relationshi. I you know the exat relationshi between jc d jc, between pp d , an on’t onsier any o that as abstrat, you then realize it is abstrat only i you haven’t ae the onnetion o obetive an subetive an seen how the atual subetive enuine huan new beinnins, whih then unite with the oveent ro theory, an ake u into this Absolute Iea   gg.
     A theory is oo or the answer o what you’re oin to o this year or
next year, but you nee an entire hilosohy or a vision o your ae’s “breakin the barrier”—that is to say, not only overthrowin the ol but reatin the new.
NOTE This iee exerts oents ae by Raya Dunayevskaya urin the 1978 Convention o News an Letters Coittees, in resonse to a question ro the loor about the eanin o ialetial hilosohy. ## 2 | Ideology

         I in all ideology men and their circumstances appear upside-down as in a
         camera obscura, this phenomenon arises just as much rom their historical
         lie-process as the inversion o objects on the retina does rom their
         physical lie-process.
         —Karl Marx, The German Ideology
         People with advantages are loath to believe that they just happen to
         be people with advantages. hey come readily to deine themselves as
         inherently worthy o what they possess; they come to believe themselves
         “naturally” elite; and, in act, to imagine their possessions and their
         privileges as natural extensions o their own elite selves. In this sense, the
         idea o the elite as composed o men and women having a iner moral
         character is an ideology o the elite as a privileged ruling stratum, and this
         is true whether the ideology is elite-made or made up or it by others.
         —C. Wright Mills, The Power Elite

2.1 Socialism or Barbarism
      C C
n most countries the working class is organized in gigantic trade unions and Ipolitical parties, numbering tens o millions o members. But these unions and parties are every day more openly and more cynically playing the role o direct agents o the ruling class and o the capitalist state, or o the bureaucratic capitalism that reigns in Russia.
    Only a ew minute organizations seem to have survived the general shipwreck, organizations such as the “Fourth International,” the Anarchist Federations, and a ew sel-described “ultraletist” groups (Bordigists, Spartacists,
Council Communists). hese organizations are very weak, not only because o their numbers (numerical strength by itsel is never a criterion), but above all because o their political and ideological bankruptcy. Relics o the past rather than harbingers o the uture, they have proved themselves utterly incapable o understanding the undamental social transormations o the twentieth century and even less capable o developing a positive orientation toward them.

## I  o o  y | 57

     In some countries, the Anarchist Federations still enjoy the support o a
number o workers with a healthy class instinct—but those workers are very backward politically, and the anarchists keep them that way. he anarchists’ constant reusal to venture beyond the sterile slogan “No Politics,” or to take theory seriously, contributes to the conusion. his makes anarchism one more blind alley or workers to get lost in.
     Meanwhile, various “ultraletist” groups cultivate their pet sectarian deviations, some o them (like the Bordigists) even going so ar as to blame the
proletariat or their own stagnation and impotence, others (like the Council Communists) living happily in the past and seeking therein their recipes or the “socialist” kitchens o the uture.
     Within the ramework o a world system based on exploitation, new
economic orms and new types o exploitation have appeared. While maintaining the most undamental eatures o capitalism these new orms dier signiicantly rom traditional capitalism in that they have superseded and broken radically with such traditional capitalist orms as the private ownership o the means o production. hese new economic orms even supericially resemble some o the objectives the workers’ movement had set itsel, objectives such as the statiication or nationalization o the means o production and exchange, economic planning and the coordination o production on an international scale.
     he bureaucracy was the social expression o these new economic orms.
As traditional orms o property and the bourgeoisie o the classical period are pushed aside by state property and by the bureaucracy, the main con- lict within society gradually ceases to be the old one between the owners o wealth and those without property and is replaced by the conlict between directors and executants in the process o production. In act, the bureaucracy justiies its own existence (and can be explained in objective terms) only insoar as it plays a role deemed essential to the “management” o the productive activities o society and, thereby, o all other orms o activity.
     his has taken place without there resulting or the toiling masses anything other than a more intense, better coordinated, and, in a word, rationalized exploitation. he objective outcome o this evolution has been a more
eicient and more systematic organization or exploiting and enslaving the proletariat.
     he beneiciary o this exploitation o the proletariat is an enormous and
monstrous bureaucracy (consisting o the bureaucrats in the political and economic apparatus, o technicians and intellectuals, o leaders o the “Communist” party and o the trade unions, and o the top military and police personnel). Economic “planning” proceeds in the interests o the bureaucracy and aects all areas o production.

## 58 | C p 2

     hus there evolved a system that cynically calls itsel “socialist” yet where
side by side with the appalling poverty o the working masses can be seen the lie o luxury led by about 10 or 15 percent o the population who make up the exploiting bureaucracy. his is a system where millions o people are held in concentration and orced labor camps, where the state police (o which the Gestapo was but an imitation) exercises total terror, where “elections” and other “democratic” procedures would be deemed sinister arces were they not the tragic expressions o the terrorization, the brutalization, and the degradation o man under the most overwhelming dictatorship alive today.
     How did things reach this pass? How did the power established by the
irst victorious proletarian revolution transorm itsel into the most eective instrument or exploiting and oppressing the masses? And how did the parties o the hird International, created to abolish exploitation and to instaurate on earth the power o workers and peasants, become the instruments o a new social ormation with interests as radically opposed to those o the proletariat as had been those o the traditional bourgeoisie itsel? hese are the questions that all advanced workers will anxiously ask themselves, once they have understood that to see anything “socialist” in Russia is to calumniate the very word socialism.
     he October Revolution succumbed to the bureaucratic counterrevolution under the combined pressures o external and internal orces, o objective
and subjective actors. hey all boil down to the ollowing idea: Between the second and third decade o this century neither the world economy nor the working class was as yet quite ripe or the total abolition o exploitation. A revolution, even a victorious one, would be overthrown i it remained isolated in a single country. It would either be overthrown rom outside, through civil war and the armed intervention o other capitalist countries, or it would degenerate rom within, through a change in the nature o the regime to which it had given birth.
     A undamental question thereore has to be answered on the morrow
o every successul revolution. Who will be the master o society once it is purged o the capitalists and their tools? he structure o the new regime, its political orm, the relationship between the working class and its own leadership, the management o production, the type o system prevailing in the actories—all these are but particular aspects o this general problem.
     Should a hird World War lead to the uniication o the world system o
exploitation, the civilization and social lie o humanity would be threatened with total collapse. he unlimited totalitarian domination o a single group o exploiters (whether Yankee monopolists or Russian bureaucrats) would give them ree rein to plunder the earth. he all in the productivity o labor under such a regime o ever-increasing exploitation and the complete transormation

## I  o  o  y | 59

o its dominant stratum into a parasitic caste no longer having any need to develop the orces o production would lead to a massive regression in social conditions and to a prolonged setback in the development o human consciousness.
NOE his piece is excerpted rom Cornelius Castoriadis, Political and Social Writings, vol. 1, 1946–1955, trans. and ed. David Ames Curtis (Minneapolis: University o Minnesota Press, 1988). Copyright 1988 by the University o Minnesota. Reprinted by permission o the publisher.

2.2 Ideology Materialized
       G D
       Sel-consciousness exists in itsel and or itsel only insoar as it exists in and or
       another sel-consciousness; that is, it exists only by being recognized.
       —.W.F. egel, The Phenomenology of Spirit
212 Ideology is the intellectual basis o class societies within the conlictual course o history. Ideological expressions have never been pure ictions; they represent a distorted consciousness o realities, and as such they have been real actors that have in turn produced real distorting eects. his interconnection is intensiied with the advent o the spectacle—the materialization o ideology brought about by the concrete success o an autonomized system o economic production—which virtually identiies social reality with an ideology that has remolded all reality in its own image.
213 Once ideology—the abstract will to universality and the illusion associated with that will—is legitimized by the universal abstraction and the eective dictatorship o illusion that prevail in modern society, it is no longer a voluntaristic struggle o the ragmentary but its triumph. At that point, ideological pretensions take on a sort o lat, positivistic precision: they no longer represent historical choices; they are assertions o undeniable acts. In such a context, the particular names o ideologies tend to disappear. he speciically ideological orms o system-supporting labor are reduced to an “epistemological base” that is itsel presumed to be beyond ideology. Materialized ideology has no name, just as it has no ormulatable historical agenda. Which is another way o saying that the history o different ideologies is over.

## 60 | C p 2

214 Ideology, whose whole internal logic led toward what [Karl] Mannheim calls “total ideology”—the despotism o a ragment imposing itsel as pseudoknowledge o a rozen totality, as a totalitarian worldview—has reached its culmination in the immobilized spectacle o nonhistory. Its culmination is also its dissolution into society as a whole. When that society itsel is concretely dissolved, ideology—the final irrationality standing in the way o historical lie—must also disappear.
215 he spectacle is the epitome o ideology because in its plenitude it exposes and maniests the essence o all ideological systems: the impoverishment, enslavement, and negation o real lie. he spectacle is the material “expression o the separation and estrangement between man and man.” he “new power o deception” concentrated in it is based on the production system in which “as the quantity o objects increases, so does the realm o alien powers to which man is subjected.” his is the supreme stage o an expansion that has turned need against lie. “he need or money is thus the true need produced by the modern economic system, and it is the only need which the latter produces.” Hegel’s characterization o money as “the lie o what is dead, moving within itsel” has now been extended by the spectacle to all social lie.
216 In contrast to the project outlined in the “heses on Feuerbach” (the realization o philosophy in a praxis transcending the opposition between idealism and materialism), the spectacle preserves the ideological eatures o both materialism and idealism, imposing them in the pseudo-concreteness o its universe. he contemplative aspect o the old materialism, which conceives the world as representation and not as activity—and which ultimately idealizes matter—is ulilled in the spectacle, where concrete things are automatic masters o social lie. Conversely, the dreamed activity o idealism is also ulilled in the spectacle, through the technical mediation o signs and signals—which ultimately materialize an abstract ideal.
217 he parallel between ideology and schizophrenia demonstrated in [Joseph] Gabel’s False Consciousness should be considered in the context o this eco-

## I  o o  y | 61

nomic materialization o ideology. Society has become what ideology already was. he racturing o practice and the antidialectical alse consciousness that results rom that racturing are imposed at every moment o everyday lie subjected to the spectacle—a subjection that systematically destroys the “aculty o encounter” and replaces it with a social hallucination: a alse consciousness o encounter, an “illusion o encounter.” In a society where no one can any longer be recognized by others, each individual becomes incapable o recognizing his own reality. Ideology is at home; separation has built its own world.
218 “In clinical accounts o schizophrenia,” says Gabel, “the deterioration o the dialectic o totality (with dissociation as its extreme orm) and the deterioration o the dialectic o becoming (with catatonia as its extreme orm) seem closely interrelated.” Imprisoned in a lattened universe bounded by the screen o the spectacle, behind which his own lie has been exiled, the spectator’s consciousness no longer knows anyone but the fictitious interlocutors who subject him to a one-way monologue about their commodities and the politics o their commodities. he spectacle as a whole is his “mirror sign,” presenting illusory escapes rom a universal autism.
219 he spectacle, which obliterates the boundaries between sel and world by crushing the sel besieged by the presence/absence o the world, also obliterates the boundaries between true and alse by repressing all directly lived truth beneath the real presence o alsehood maintained by the organization o appearances. Individuals who passively accept their subjection to an alien everyday reality are thus driven toward a madness that reacts to that ate by resorting to illusory magical techniques. he essence o this pseudo-response to an unanswerable communication is the acceptance and consumption o commodities. he consumer’s compulsion to imitate is a truly inantile need, conditioned by all the aspects o his undamental dispossession. As Gabel puts it in describing a quite dierent level o pathology, “he abnormal need or representation here makes up or a torturing eeling o being on the edge o existence.”
220 In contrast to the logic o alse consciousness, which cannot truly know itsel, the search or critical truth about the spectacle must also be a true critique.

## 62 | C p 2

It must struggle in practice among the irreconcilable enemies o the spectacle and admit that it is nothing without them. By rushing into sordid reormist compromises or pseudo-revolutionary collective actions, those driven by an abstract desire or immediate eectiveness are in reality obeying the ruling laws o thought, adopting a perspective that can see nothing but the latest news. In this way delirium reappears within the camp that claims to be opposing it. A critique seeking to go beyond the spectacle must know how to wait.
221 he sel-emancipation o our time is an emancipation rom the material bases o inverted truth. his “historic mission o establishing truth in the world” can be carried out neither by the isolated individual nor by atomized and manipulated masses but only and always by the class that is able to dissolve all classes by reducing all power to the de-alienating orm o realized democracy—to councils in which practical theory veriies itsel and surveys its own actions. Only there are individuals “directly linked to world history”—there where dialogue has armed itsel to impose its own conditions.
NOE his piece was previously published as Guy Debord, “Ideology Materialized,” in The Society of the Spectacle, trans. Ken Knabb, available at http://bopsecrets.org/SI/debord/9.htm.

     1. Karl Mannheim, Ideology and Utopia (San Diego, CA: Harcourt Brace, 1936), 57.
     2. Karl Marx, “Human Requirements and Division o Labour under the Rule o Private
Property,” in Economic and Philosophical Manuscripts of 1844 (Moscow: Progress, 1959), available at https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1844/manuscripts/needs.htm.
     3. G.W.F. Hegel, Jenaer Realphilosophie, vol. 1, Die Vorlesungen von 1803/4, ed. J. Ho-
meister (Leipzig, Germany: Felix Meiner, 1932), 239–240.
     4. Joseph Gabel, La fausse conscience: Essai sur la réication (Paris: Éditions de Minuit,
1962).
     5. Ibid.

2.3 American “Common Sense”
         F P
MICn “CoMMon SnS”
      - It’s impossible or people to run their own lives; that’s why they
         don’t have the power to do so. People are powerless because they
         have neither the ability nor the desire to control and decide about
         the social and material conditions in which they live.

## I  o  o  y | 63

   - People only want power and privileges over each other. It would
     be impossible, or example, or university students to ight against
     the institution that assures them a privileged position. hose students who study do so to get high grades, because with the high
     grades they can get high-paying jobs, which means the ability to
     manage and manipulate other people and the ability to buy more
     consumer goods than other people. I learning were not rewarded
     with high grades, high pay, power over others, and lots o goods, no
     one would learn; there’d be no motivation or learning.
   - It would be just as impossible or workers to want to run their actories, to want to decide about their production. All that workers are
     interested in is wages: they just want more wages than others have,
     so as to buy bigger houses, more cars, and longer trips.
   - Even i students, workers, armers wanted something dierent,
     they’re obviously satisied with what they’re doing; otherwise they
     wouldn’t be doing it.
   - In any case, those who aren’t satisied can reely express their dissatisaction by buying and by voting: they don’t have to buy the
     things they don’t like, and they don’t have to vote or the candidates
     they don’t like. It’s impossible or them to change their situation
     any other way.
   - Even i some people tried to change the situation some other way,
     it would be impossible or them to get together; they’d only ight
     each other, because white workers are racists, black nationalists are
     antiwhite, eminists are against all men, and students have their
     own speciic problems.
   - Even i they did unite, it would obviously be impossible or them to
     destroy the state and the police and military potential o a powerul
     industrial society like the United States.
“SCInIFIC BSIS” oF  “CoMMon SnS” A “social scientist” is someone who is paid to deend this society’s myths. His deense mechanism, in its simplest ormulation, runs approximately as ollows: he begins by assuming that the society o his time and place is the only possible orm o society; he then concludes that some other orm o society is impossible. Unortunately, the “social scientist” rarely admits his assumptions; he usually claims that he doesn’t make any assumptions. And it can’t be said that he’s lying outright: he usually takes his assumptions so much or granted that he doesn’t even know he’s making them.

## 64 | C p 2

     he “social scientist” claims to be empirical and objective; he claims to
make no value judgments. Yet by reducing the person to the bundle o tastes, desires, and preerences to which he’s restricted in capitalist society, the “objective scientist” makes the bizarre claim that this bundle is what the worker is; and he makes the antastic value judgment that the worker cannot be other than what he is in capitalist society. According to the “laws o human behavior” o this “science,” the solidarity o students with workers, the occupation o actories by workers, the desire o workers to run their own production, distribution, and coordination, are all impossible. Why? Because these things are impossible in capitalist society, and or these “scientists” who make no value judgments, existing societies are the only possible societies, and the corporate-military society is the best o all possible societies.
     o the American social scientist, “human nature” is what people do in
corporate-military America: a ew make decisions, and the rest ollow orders; some think, and others do; some buy other people’s labor, and the rest sell their own labor; a ew invest, and the rest are consumers; some are sadists, and others masochists; some have a desire to kill and others to die. he “scientist” passes all this o as “exchange,” as “reciprocity,” as a “division o labor” in which people are divided along with tasks. o the “social scientist” this is all so natural that he thinks he makes no value judgments when he takes it all or granted. Corporations and the military even give him grants to show that it’s always been this way: grants to demonstrate that this “human nature” is lodged in the beginning o history and in the depths o the unconscious. (American psychologists—especially “behaviorists”—make the ambiguous “contribution” o demonstrating that animals also have a “human nature”: the psychologists drive rats mad in a situation similar to a war, which the psychologists themselves helped plan, and then they show that rats, too, have a desire to kill, that they have masochist tendencies.)
     In terms o what the American “social scientist” takes or granted, when
students and workers in France started to ight to do away with “reciprocity,” “exchange,” and the division o labor, they were ighting not against the capitalist police but against “human nature.” And since this is obviously impossible, the events that took place in May 1968 did not take place.
“CoMMon SnS” poS he question o what is possible cannot be answered in terms o what is.
    o keep its relative privileges, each group tries to keep the groups below
rom shaking the structure.
    hus in times o “peace” the system is largely sel-policed: the colonized
repress the colonized, blacks repress blacks, whites repress each other, the

## I  o o  y | 65

blacks, and the colonized. hus the working population represses itsel, “law and order” is maintained, and the ruling class is saved rom urther outlays on the repressive apparatus.
    o the “social scientist” and the proessional propagandist, this “division
o labor” is as natural as “human nature” itsel. Unity among the dierent “interest groups” is as inconceivable to the “social scientist” as revolution.
    While holding as “scientiically proved” that the dierent groups cannot
unite in an anticapitalist struggle, the expert does all he can to prevent such unity, and his colleagues design weapons just in case people did unite against the capitalist system.
    Because sometimes the whole structure cracks.
    he same expert who deines the capitalist system as consistent with
“human nature,” with people’s tastes, wishes, desires, constructs the arsenal o myths and weapons with which the system deends itsel. But what does the system deend itsel against: human nature? I it has to ight against human nature to survive, then by the expert’s own language, the system is extremely unnatural.
    hus while some experts deine the rebellion in France as impossible because unnatural, their expert colleagues design the incapacitating gases with
which cops can suppress such impossible rebellions.    .
NOE his essay is excerpted rom Fredy Perlman, Anything Can Happen (1968; repr., London: Phoenix, 1992).

2.4 Radical Learning through Neoliberal Crisis
       S R
       Education is liberation, removal o all weeds, rubble, and vermin that seek to harm
       the plant’s delicate shoots, a radiance o light and warmth, the loving rush o rain
       alling at night.
       —Friedrich nietzsche, “Schopenhauer as Educator”
SMMy Liberalism reconciles capitalism and democracy in the humanist cultivation o moral and sel-improving individuals. Markets and elections require identical capacities: discerning intellect, empathic recognition, agonistic contention, ethical invigilation, and desire to contribute individual to collective

## 66 | C p 2

progress. Liberal discourse integrates diverse opinions by nurturing private skills and public duties, personal dreams and civic ainities. Liberal education supports this ideological repression o capitalist-democratic tensions; its neurotic pedagogical subjects instantiate the rickety bad-aith antasy o the liberal-democratic peace.
     Neoliberalism radicalizes capitalism by shedding this integument o humanist pastoralism with its political critique o liberal consolation. Neoliberalism disburdens liberalism o its obsolete palliatives, replacing its docile,
disciplined, civic-minded citizens with entrepreneurial sel-investors eager to sacriice everything or market society. Capitalism is wrested rom antiquated humanitarian etters o welare economics and inantilizing therapies. Neoliberal education enorces this ideological repression o communal mediation; its psychotic subjects instantiate the bad-aith antasy o turning social aggression into capitalist holism.
     Neoliberalism’s opponents ight antihumanist hypercapitalism with anticapitalist hyperhumanism. I urge instead a dialectical inversion o neoliberal
discourse that reuses the alse comorts o bourgeois democracy. aking U.S. higher education as a neoliberal exemplar, I envisage an audacious pedagogy that reclaims the generalized physical courage, personal sacriice, unconscious desires, and “motivating” terror o precarious lie to transcend the repressive antasies o both liberalism and neoliberalism.
IBISM It is oten said that European philosophy is ounded on the death sentence o a teacher, Socrates, convicted o corrupting youth and ignoring gods. A harsh sentence or a teacher who “never promised to teach . . . anything,” a knower who knew only that “I do not think I know what I do not know.” “Corrupting youth” meant insulating their innocence rom artiice and conceit—indeed rom corruption.
     Liberalism draws on blank slates, guided by a similar “romantic notion
o original innocence [and] childhood as a period o wisdom, purity, and creativity, a stage . . . children should be let to enjoy rather than be disciplined out o.” Humanist pedagogy rejected the “rote memorization, relentless drills, endless repetition, daily analysis o texts, elaborate exercises in imitation and rhetorical variation, all backed up by the threat o violence,” which were characteristic in Shakespeare’s day. Children and students would embody the liberal order that educated them: hopeul, curious, playul, restless, excited, and brimming with sel-discovery.
     But anxiety lurks in the “innocence [and] childhood [o our] democratic
ideal.” Innocence orges liberal morality, and its etishized violation justiies

## I  o o  y | 67

it. We seek out harrowing stories o abused children to airm our primal innocence. “Stolen youth” consecrates this purity: a boy’s “happy, careree years o childhood . . . drawn to a close” by war; a girl let “no longer a child” by occupation; a kid aged to death by poverty. But it also sublimates our anxiety that we are not innocent. Liberal humanists blame the outside world or our allen selves but also our inner selves or our allen world. hese ambivalent selves converge in the conlicted pedagogy o liberal “reedom” that tames children and capitalists alike.
     In The Bell Jar Sylvia Plath sneers, “I was taking one o those honors
programs that teach you to think independently.” One thinks or others and only then or onesel. Liberalism irst must tame the “wild child” released by sel-exploration. “hese ‘vagabonds o the intellect’” loosed by humanism’s “vast operation o de-consecration” must be contained. Novels about college assured us that narcissism would replace the “inner-direction implanted early in lie by the elders.” But education theorists like Ernest Becker lamented the “tragic paradox [o] education and the state”: “liberal education . . . train[ed] youth to exercise their own judgement, seek their own solutions, criticize and master the world on their own term. [But then recoiled] that they [might] criticize . . . anything.” Liberal pedagogy inevitably echoed Immanuel Kant’s injunction to “argue as much as you like and about whatever you like, but obey!” “Intellectual reedom” requires limits on “civil reedom,” the theory went, just as democracy should limit capitalism.
     Education served the utilitarian needs o the “civilizing process” but especially o capitalism. Democratic pedagogy was “to provide the modern state
with enlightened citizens and to train an eicient work orce.” Liberal schooling also ensured wealthy classes a “rate o return on educational capital.” But capitalist-democratic cultivation o individuality and wage labor were unstable. his tension recalls Karl Marx’s chiasmus: “In bourgeois society capital is independent and has individuality, while the living person is dependent and has no individuality.” Exposing this ideological perversion, heodor Adorno would stress that the “precarious and irrational sel-preservation o society is alsiied and turned into an achievement o its immanent justice or ‘rationality.’” Humanist capitalism exempliies this process.
     Liberal pedagogy supports the democratic claim to promote civil society
to “restrict the private abuse o public institutions” and “prevent aristocratic tendencies.” Children must become little liberals prepared or enlightened civic “participation.” Humanism advances the “consciously socialized interest,” in John Dewey’s phrase, against “customs operating under the control o a superior class.” Even playgrounds were “arenas or democratic advancement [o] children rich and poor, immigrant and native-born.” he liberal student is, in short, a welter o capitalist-democratic subjectivities. She works

## 68 | C p 2

hard; reads and votes critically; becomes a CEO or protests one; joins or resists a war—all within the humanist-pedagogic ethos. She knows that rights entail duties, privilege requires civics, and social reciprocity reconciles entrepreneur and elector in a stable capitalist polity in which “the ree assent o the members is the only source o political authority.” Liberalism:

     - nurtures authentic selhood
     - develops personal character
     - assimilates individual desires
     - integrates idiosyncratic creativity
     - cultivates particular talents
     - aligns unique pursuits with general ethics
     - inculcates “agency” discourse
     - disciplines excess energies
     - sublimates disruptive desires/aects
     - moralizes communal sentiment
     - instrumentalizes reined talents
     - establishes logocentric valuation
     - internalizes implicit social codes
     - consolidates ideological conormity
     - diuses public obligation
     - antasizes sel/society reconciliation

noIBISM Neoliberalism decimates humanist solace in a capitalism without consolation, apology, or guilt, populated by sel-investors without nagging superegos or nodding therapists. Neoliberalism is terrorism, instilling ear o abjection under market austerity. Neoliberalism is to liberalism as Leninism is to Marxism, reviving a gradualist theory by attacking its etters. Immanent in the liberalism it condemns, neoliberalism commodiies education to promote paranoid aggression. hrough its academic outposts, neoliberalism imposes an “inverted totalitarian” kingdom o means.
    Neoliberalism has been a pedagogical calamity. First, increasing poverty,
inequity, and inancial burdens devastate students, teachers, and campuses. Meretricious spending on vanity projects and iscal austerity combine to deund public schools, triggering social migration; prioritize athletic over academic programs; encourage elite colleges to exclude poorer applicants; increase dropout rates through debt peonage; and incur living-wage campaigns by sta. Corporate higher education attacks aculty as well by intensiying battles over intellectual property, productivity assessments, cutthroat

## I  o o  y | 69

careerism, precarious employment, and poverty-wage adjunct assignments. Second, neoliberal inancial strangulation subsists on a steady discursive diet o meritocracy, lean production, business modeling, test-prep preoccupation, and “learning outcome” yardsticks. In eect bourgeois pedagogy celebrates SEM-centric science envy, denigrates literature and the humanities, hinders open inquiry and innovative research, harms teaching and undermines learning, and erodes academic reedom.
     Many misconstrue neoliberal harshness as capitalism on steroids: a degree
change in bourgeois economics. Neoliberalism advances not by reinventing capitalism but by removing its social emollients. Seeing neoliberalism as extreme capitalism romanticizes its earlier incarnations and eclipses resistance to them. Neoliberalism assails dissent rom maximal capitalist exploitation. As Samir Amin says, “he weakening o the working class and historic let stance . . . encourages capital to take a so-called neoliberal hard line.” he supposed indices o neoliberalism have been here all along: disposable people, reserve workers, class conlict, systemic precariousness, personal responsibility, and capital-subservient pedagogy. Liberalism reers not to the lack o bourgeois rigors but o their public amelioration. Its neoliberal successor puriies capitalism by destroying humane restraint along with its antasy o harmonious capitalist lie.
     “he resurgence o liberalism in the orm o neoliberalism” is a normative correction; it rewires the democratic asylum, not the capitalist lunatic.
First, “neoliberalism [constitutes] a rationality” o “eective abandonment.” We live or die by our own exertion. Second, neoliberalism ully subsumes rather than just commodiies. hings and people aren’t potentially but necessarily reduced to exchange values and punished or nonmarket pursuits. hird, capitalist markets are organic systems requiring the sacriice o personal security and communal reciprocity. Neoliberalism replaces repressive ideology with sacriicial theology, recalling Søren Kierkegaard’s denial o a “guarantee that our sacriice will be rewarded, that it will restore Meaning to our lie—we have to make a leap o aith which, to an external observer cannot but look like an act o madness.” Wendell Berry calls it “sentimental capitalism,” in which “everything small, local, private, personal, natural, good, and beautiul must be sacriiced in the interest o the ‘ree market’ and the great corporations, which will bring . . . security and happiness . . . in, o course, the uture.”
     For neoliberals capitalism is the condition o reedom, supreme over individuals. hus Ludwig von Mises could call “the capitalist system o production . . . an economic democracy in which every penny gives a right to vote.”
Neoliberal governance secures property rights, enorces law, and directs markets. o Friedrich von Hayek, “[liberalism’s] deect was not that it adhered

## 70 | C p 2

too stubbornly to principles, but rather that it lacked principles suiciently deinite to provide clear guidance.” Markets need muscles—the state must compel capitalism, not just clear a space or it. Neoliberalism modiies laissez- aire economics much as state-led growth revised “ree trade.” In its “move toward more state by less government,” in Michel Foucault’s phrase, neoliberals consolidate a “neoclassical synthesis [that uses] macroeconomic policy [to] stabilize aggregate demand, utilizing tools o iscal and monetary policy.” Neoliberalism replaces:

     - authenticity with lexibility
     - personal with corporate character
     - assimilated with deregulated desires
     - idiosyncrasy with brand name
     - particular with substitutable talents
     - ethical with contractual obligation
     - agency with autonomy
     - disciplined with released excess energies
     - sublimated with expulsive antasies
     - neurotic with psychotic subjectivity
     - communitarian sentiment with enclave mentality
     - mediated with immediate gratiication
     - logocentric valuation with identitarian/psychic injury
     - implicit social codes with explicit social symbolism
     - ideological conormity with postideological candor
     - public saety with private security
     - reconciliation o sel/society with antinomy

## poSnoIB pSon()S

         But tho’ education be disclaim’d by philosophy, as a allacious ground o assent to
         any opinion, it prevails nevertheless in the world, and in this cause why all systems
         are apt to be rejected at rst as new and unusual.
         —avid ume, A Treatise of Human Nature
Rejections o neoliberalism tend toward nostalgia. I neoliberalism is “altering” under “structural strains . . . it [can] no longer . . . handle,” perhaps we should relitigate social democracy. Maybe neoliberal commodiication should inspire resistance by “cosmopolitan education” or “slow scholarship.” Because humanism limited capitalist excess, it seems natural to think a “hyperhumanism” can constrain neoliberal “hypercapitalism.” But this just the old liberal antasy recycled. Instead, let’s go through neoliberalism, explode

## I  o o  y | 71

its cracks, and reweaponize its tools. As Richard Gilman-Opalsky proclaims, crisis desires a “revolt [that] embodies and relects the transormative aspirations o everyday people.” On and o campus we must repoliticize pedagogy, revising our scholarship while turning “traditional disciplinary boundaries” into “sites/cites o contestation [in] orging . . . alternatives as academics, grassroots organizers, and activists.”
    Recall that neoliberal bad aith exposes liberal bad aith with unusual
candor about capitalism:

    1. Capitalism cannot be palliated by humanism.
    2. Capitalism cannot be softened by ideology.
    3. Capitalism is an unmediated physical test.
    4. Capitalist innovation violates tradition.
    5. Capitalist sacriices or sel equal sacriices or capital.
    6. he capitalist invests in a substitutable sel.
    7. Capitalist risk is universal.
    8. Capitalist precariousness prompts ierce resistance.

hese neoliberal commitments provide the consciousness to reject liberal democracy and the subjectivity to reject neoliberalism. We can dialectically invert and sublate neoliberal candor. Capitalism is inescapably brutal, says the neoliberal (1–3), so approach it radically and absolutely; yes, we reject it absolutely and radically. Capitalism rewards startling inventions by sacriicial members o a universal community who iercely resist precariousness, says the neoliberal (4–8); yes, therein lies the communist revolt! May the perverse play o the psychical-physical person(a) inspire the uncaptured pedagogy o the uprising.
    In Otto de Kat’s novella The Figure in the Distance, the narrator recalls
his high school perormance in Antigone: “Lie would never again have the same vastness as it had on those bare boards. . . . From that moment on, everything seemed less important. . . . He had participated in a drama o love and ate and death.” he child transorms himsel through an act, a role, a part. An epigraph to obias Wol’s This Boy’s Life is a similar comment by Oscar Wilde: “he irst duty in lie is to assume a pose. What the second is, no one has yet discovered.” he boy Wol, playing with his gun, wonders what an approaching nun “would . . . make o me. . . . I didn’t understand it mysel. Being so close to so much robust identity made me eel the poverty o my own, the ludicrous aspect o my costume and props.” Imagining himsel a sniper, the happy child is suddenly shattered, sees himsel through another as a boy. Similarly, contemplating the ducks in Catcher in the Rye, ageless nature beyond him, Holden Caulield undergoes “a conscious and voluntary

## 72 | C p 2

surrender that is prelude to Enlightenment.” How does the ecstasy o role play bring us the joy o our higher true sel?
     “Persona originally means ‘mask’ and it is through the mask that the individual acquires a role and a social identity,” Giorgio Agamben says, describing
a patrician’s insignia. Persona becomes “the ‘personality’ that deines the place o the individual in the dramas and rituals o social lie. Eventually persona came to signiy the juridical capacity and political dignity o the ree man.” “he struggle or recognition is,” he stresses, “a struggle or a mask but this mask coincides with the ‘personality’ that society recognizes in every individual.” Hence “the moral person constitutes himsel . . . through, at once, an adhesion to and a distancing rom, the social mask.” We have here a reversal o our liberal-therapeutic holistic sel. he mask as person(a) allows us to play not on our surace but beneath it, secure, unseen. hink o the director Jacques Copeau’s rustration with an actor who “could experience but not express an emotion,” the demand that he “struggle with his own blood.” Having per- ected the person(a) o his character he must imbue it with his own person: he traverses the irreducibly liminal space o the sel/person(a). his person(a) turns the interchangeable neoliberal subject, ever malleable to market direction, into a masked ugitive, revealed by will and choice alone. eachers and students should cultivate this person(a) as both intensiication and protection o creative upheaval beyond liberal conormity and neoliberal promiscuity.
     Rainer Maria Rilke’s letters (1903–1904) to Franz Kappus, a soldier seeking advice on his poetry, oer cryptic counsel. Rilke begins his mentorship
by denying its possibility: “You ask whether your verses are any good. You ask me. You have asked others beore this. . . . I beg you to stop doing that sort o thing.” His gentle pedagogy is strictly staged. First, achieve “vast, inner solitude”: “walk inside yoursel and meet no one or hours,” gazing “upon it all as a child would.” Only in radical solitude do we ind our person. Second, engage “your innermost eeling, in your quietest hour” so “the natural growth o your inner lie will eventually guide you.” hird, perceive the relationship to creativity; “ask yoursel in the most silent hour o your night: must I write?” his stage also occasions your “search into the depths o hings,” to “ind out i this way o perceiving the world arises rom a necessity o your being.” Fourth, Rilke says one must accept that “in important matters we are unspeakably alone”—the condition o natality.
     Most striking is his insistence that poetic words seek what eludes words.
“Most experiences are unsayable,” he readily announces. You must “let each impression and each embryo o a eeling come to completion, entirely in itsel, in the dark, in the unsayable, the unconscious.” What language desires, he ruminates, “is so very delicate, is almost unsayable.” When Gilles Deleuze oddly reers to “Rilke reusing to give any advice to a young poet,” he may

## I  o o  y | 73

mean his initial warning was sincere: poetic guidance itsel may be unsayable. But Rilke submits to the wordless unconscious, the process o becoming his inner experience. At this moment, one is not liberal subject o a sel but posthumanist object o a lie. In Deleuze’s words:
    It’s a strange business, speaking or yoursel, in your own name, because it doesn’t at all come with seeing yoursel as an ego or a person or a subject. Individuals ind a real name or themselves, rather,
    only through the harshest exercise in depersonalization, by opening
    themselves up to the multiplicities everywhere within them, to the
    intensities running through them.
Or in Guy Debord’s exhortation, “In a dérive one or more persons during a certain period drop their relations, their work and leisure activities, and all their other usual motives or movement and action, and let themselves be drawn by the attractions o the terrain and the encounters they end there.” When we inally recognize our persona as our misrecognized person, as the stage o our liberal subjectivity, we may experience ourselves genuinely as objects o objects, causes o causes, at home in our bodily becoming. his artistic-philosophical urge tracks to “Montaigne’s method,” that “peculiar orm o his Essays” in which “he ollows his own inner rhythm, which, though constantly induced and maintained by things, is not bound to them, but reely skips rom one to another.” Michel de Montaigne’s ounding humanist rejection o sel-mastering piety may ound a posthumanist rejection o neoliberalism’s equally sel-mutilating theology.
ICIC o A pedagogical revolt through neoliberal inhumanity welcomes to our campuses—those porous enclosures o sel-serious cerebration—the means o their remaking: laughter, love, play, anxiety, acedia, tricksters, satire, aggression, ressentiment, communism—above all a idelity to what eludes language. I a radical pedagogy is a chimera, we ought to imagine it or that very reason. We need irst, not last, to crack open the walls o the universities, classrooms, all plodding disciplinary routines now rendered reactionary. Should we disrupt our classes and ideologies with the aective silences and perceptive secrets o ugitive person(a)s, the threat posed to neoliberalism will ward o liberal retrenchment as well.
     In his memoir Paul Feyerabend says, “I think many young people . . .
see the world in a special way, and yet the slightest pressure can make them see it dierently. A good teacher respects this instability.” And nurtures it in

## 74 | C p 2

the revolutionary spirit o all “young people.” He then remembers “Oswald homas, a well-known igure in Viennese adult education. Once a month, homas assembled about two thousand people in a large meadow outside Vienna, turned o the streetlights, and explained the constellations. He told scientiic details about the stars and somewhat less scientiic stories about the legendary animals, gods, humans, that populated the sky.”

## 3 | Class Composition and Hierarchy

         All animals are equal, but some animals are more equal than others.
         —George Orwell, Animal Farm
         The market has always been one of exploitation and thereby of
         domination, insuring the class structure of society. However, the
         productive process of advanced capitalism has altered the form of
         domination: the technological veil covers the brute presence and the
         operation of the class interest in the merchandise.
         —Herbert Marcuse, An Essay on Liberation

3.1 Karl Marx’s Model of the Class Society
      R D
TH SOi TyMOOGy Of TH OpT Of SS Evaluative shifts of meaning have accompanied the concept of class throughout its history. When the Roman censors introduced the word classis to divide the population into tax groups, they may not have anticipated the eventful future of this category. Yet even their classification implied at least the possibility of evaluative distinctions: on the one end of their classification were the assidui, who might well be proud of their 100,000 as; on the other end were the proletarii, whose only “property” consisted in their numerous offspring—proles—and who were outdone only by the lumpenproletariat of the capite censi, those counted by their heads. Just as the American term “income bracket,” although originally no more than a statistical category, touches upon the most vulnerable point of social inequality, it was true for the classes of ancient Rome that they divided the population into more than statistical units. “The movie was classy,” teenagers say, meaning “high-class,” “firstclass.” Similarly, to say that some Roman was classis or classicus meant that he belonged to the prima classis, to the upper class—unless he was explicitly described as a “fifth-class” proletarian. Since Gellius we know the adjective classicus in its application to “first-class” artists and works of art, a usage which
                                          S S  OM p O S i T iO    H i     H y | 79
survives in our word “classical” and was eventually related to the authors of the term themselves and their times: they lived in “classical” antiquity.
     When more recently sociologists remembered the word, they naturally
gave it a slightly different connotation. Initially the word “class” was used— for example, by [Adam] Ferguson and [John] Millar in the eighteenth century—simply to distinguish social strata, as we should say today, by their rank or wealth. In this sense the word “class” can be found in all European languages in the late eighteenth century. In the nineteenth century the concept of class gradually took on a more definite coloring. Adam Smith had already spoken of the “poor” or “laboring class.” In the works of [David] Ricardo and [Andrew] Ure, [Henri de] Saint-Simon and [Charles] Fourier, and of course in those of [Friedrich] Engels and [Karl] Marx the “class of capitalists” makes its appearance beside the “laboring class,” the “rich” beside the “poor class,” the “bourgeoisie” beside the “proletariat” (which has accompanied the concept of class from its Roman origins). Since this particular concept of social class was first applied in the middle of the nineteenth century, its history has been as eventful as that of the society for which it was designed.
     Indeed, the greatness and fatality of his work become apparent in Marx’s
theory of class. In this theory, the three roots of his thought are joined. Marx adopted the word from the early British political economists; its application to “capitalists” and “proletarians” stems from the French “utopian” socialists; the conception of the class struggle is based on [Georg] Hegel’s dialectics. The theory of class provides the problematic link between sociological analysis and philosophical speculation in the work of Marx. Both can be separated, and have to be separated, but in this process the theory of class is cut in two; for it is as essential for Marx’s philosophy of history as it is for his analysis of the dynamics of capitalist society.
     Marx postponed the systematic presentation of his theory of class until
death took the pen from his hand. The irony has often been noted that the last (fifty-second) chapter of the last (third) volume of Capital, which bears the title “The Classes,” has remained unfinished. After little more than one page the text ends with the lapidary remark of its editor, Engels: “Here the manuscript breaks off.”
     The following elements of Marx’s theory of class appear particularly
worth emphasizing for sociological analysis:
     It is important to realize what [Theodor] Geiger called the “heuristic purpose behind the concept of class.” Wherever Marx used the concept in a sociological sense, he was not concerned with describing an existing state of society.
He was concerned, rather, with the analysis of certain laws of social development and of the forces involved in this development. To use the misleading terms of

## 80 | H pT 3

modern sociology, the heuristic purpose of the concept of class was for Marx not “static” but “dynamic,” not “descriptive” but “analytical.” What these terms may mean and what they cannot mean will have to be discussed later in some detail. Here it is sufficient to emphasize that for Marx the theory of class was not a theory of a cross-section of society arrested in time, in particular not a theory of social stratification, but a tool for the explanation of changes in total societies.
     This heuristic purpose explains the often criticized two-class model underlying the dynamic theory of Marx. Had Marx wanted to describe his
society with photographic accuracy, this model would indeed have been most unsatisfactory. As a matter of fact, Marx does refer occasionally (without always using his concept of class in an entirely unambiguous manner) to a multitude of classes. He refers to the “two great categories into which the interest of the bourgeoisie is divided—land ownership and capital,” to the petty bourgeoisie as a “transitional class,” and to the class of small peasants.
     But the legitimacy of assuming for analytical purposes the dominance
of only two conflicting classes must not blind us to the fact that Marx has linked with his two-class model a number of additional postulates whose legitimacy appears rather more dubious. For Marx the category of class defines one side of an antagonism which entails the dominant issues of conflict in every society as well as the direction of its development. This means for Marx that (a) every conflict capable of generating structural change is a class conflict, (b) the contents of class conflict always represent the dominant issues of social conflict, and (c) the two classes stand in the relation of Hegel’s “thesis” and “antithesis,” in the sense that one is characterized by the affirmation (or possession) of those features of which the other is the complete negation.
     Marx states quite clearly that class conflicts do not originate in differences
of income, or of the sources of income. His classes are not tax classes in the sense of the Roman censors. Rather, the determinant of classes is “property.” Property, however, must not be understood in terms of purely passive wealth, but as an effective force of production, as “ownership of means of production” and its denial to others. In this sense, the “relations of production”—i.e., the authority relations resulting from the distribution of effective property in the realm of (industrial) production—constitute the ultimate determinant of the formation of classes and the development of class conflicts. The capitalists possess factories and machines, and buy the only property of the proletarians, their labor power, in order to produce a surplus value with these means of production and augment their capital. But our question cannot be answered all that easily. The role of property in Marx’s theory of class poses a problem of interpretation, and on this interpretation the validity of Marx’s theory of class stands or falls. Does Marx understand, by the relations of property or production, the relations of factual control and subordination in the enter-
                                          S S  OM p O S i T iO    H i     H y | 81
prises of industrial production—or merely the authority relations insofar as they are based on the legal title of property? Does he conceive of property in a loose (sociological) sense—i.e., in terms of the exclusiveness of legitimate control (in which the manager also exercises property functions)—or merely as a statutary property right in connection with such control? Is property for Marx a special case of authority—or, vice versa, authority a special case of property? These questions are of considerable significance. If one works with the narrow concept of property, class conflict is the specific characteristic of a form of production which rests on the union of ownership and control. In this case a society in which control is exercised, for example, by state functionaries, has by definition neither classes nor class conflicts. If, on the other hand, one works with the wider concept of property, class structure is determined by the authority structure of the enterprise, and the category of class becomes at least potentially applicable to all “relations of production.”
     Marx does not always make his answer to our questions entirely clear. But
it can be shown that his analyses are essentially based on the narrow, legal concept of property. This procedure, and this procedure only, enables Marx to link his sociology with his philosophy of history—a brilliant attempt, but at the same time a fault that robs his sociological analyses of stringency and conviction, a fault made no more acceptable by the fact that orthodox Marxists have remained faithful to their master in this point to the present day.
     One of the critical pivots of Marx’s theory of class is the undisputed identification of economic and political power and authority. Although classes are
founded on the “relations of production”—i.e., the distribution of effective property in the narrow sphere of commodity production—they become socially significant only in the political sphere. But both these spheres are inseparable. “The political power” of a class arises for Marx “from the relations of production.” The relations of production are “the final secret, the hidden basis of the whole construction of society”; industrial classes are eo ipso also social classes, and industrial class conflict is political class conflict. Nowhere has Marx explicitly discussed the basis of this empirical proposition—nor has he seen sufficiently clearly that it is an empirical proposition rather than a postulate or premise. The thesis that political conditions are determined by industrial conditions seems to stem, for him, from the generalized assertion of an absolute and universal primacy of production over all other structures of economy and society.
     Classes do not constitute themselves as such until they participate in
political conflicts as organized groups. Although Marx occasionally uses the concept of class in a less determinate, more comprehensive sense, a multitude of statements leave little doubt that for him class formation and class conflict were phenomena belonging to the sphere of politics. “As long as the proletariat has not sufficiently developed to organize itself as a class, . . . the

## 82 | H pT 3

struggle of the proletariat with the bourgeoisie has not yet assumed a political character.” This means conversely that the carriers of class conflict have organized themselves as classes, and have become classes, only if class conflict has assumed a political character.
     The work of Marx falls into two separable parts. [One part is scientific,
based on sociology, and the other is based on philosophy that is unprovable.] Marx’s theory of class contains elements of both kinds. Indeed, nowhere has Marx linked both kinds of propositions as cleverly, and hence as deceptively, as in his theory of class.
     In a logically independent approach Marx investigates the society of his
time. There he notes empirically three factors, among others: (a) the presence of a conflict between social groups (classes), (b) the presence of effective private property, and (c) the presence of relations of domination and subjection. He believes furthermore that he can discern in this society a tendency for private property to be abolished and replaced by communal property—an observation which at least to some extent has proved correct. And what happens if effective private property disappears? It is precisely at this point that Marx jumps from sociology into philosophy and back by introducing his undoubtedly brilliant trick of definition. By asserting the dependence of classes on relations of domination and subjection, and the dependence of these relations on the possession of or exclusion from effective private capital, he makes on the one hand empirically private property and on the other hand philosophically social classes, the central factor of his analyses. One can retrace step by step the thought process to which Marx has succumbed at this point. It is not the thought process of the empirical scientist who seeks only piecemeal knowledge and expects only piecemeal progress but that of the system builder who suddenly finds that everything fits! For if private property disappears (empirical hypothesis), then there are no longer classes (trick of definition)! If there are no longer any classes, there is no alienation (speculative postulate). The realm of liberty is realized on earth (philosophical idea). Had Marx, conversely, defined private property by authority relations, his empirical observation would not have “fitted,” and he would have had to drop his philosophy of history. For effective private property may disappear empirically, but authority relations can do so only by the magic trick of the system maniac.
     Perhaps a Marx without the Marxian philosophy of history would have
realized that power and authority are not tied to the legal title of property. Marx himself could not realize this, and certainly could not admit it, for had he done so, his philosophical conception of the classless society would have become impossible both empirically and intellectually.
     There is, moreover, a strange irony in the fact that the same Marx who
so often attacked the uncritical assertion that private property is universal
                                           S S  OM p O S i T iO    H i     H y | 83
introduces the same assertion in a concealed way but equally uncritically by speaking of the universality of classes, which for him are tied to the presence of private property.
     A society is classless if it is “powerless”—i.e., if there is no authority exercised in it at all, or if such authority is distributed equally among all citizens.
But in this sense the category of classless society is sociologically meaningless. It may be possible to conceive of a society in which all differences of income and prestige are leveled and which is therefore “stratumless,” but it is hardly possible to imagine a society in which there is no differentiation of roles in terms of legitimate power. Permanent anarchism is socially Utopian. Any society, and, indeed, any social organization, requires some differentiation into positions of domination and positions of subjection. No matter what the formal nature of the authority mechanism, it is a functional imperative of social organizations. Since classes can be explained in terms of the differential distribution of authority, there is no sociological substance in the assumption of a classless society devoid of differentiated authority structures.
     However, the idea of a classless society may be understood in a second
sense. It is possible to conceive of a society whose structure contains positions equipped with different authority rights but which does not enable any group of persons to occupy these positions regularly and exclusively. The same might hold in imperatively coordinated associations other than the state—e.g., in industry. Associations may be governed by the principle of an alternating chairmanship, according to which the incumbency of positions of domination may or may not be patterned. The collective settlements (kibbutzim) of Israel seem to provide a case in point. At least originally it was stipulated that every member in turn was to occupy the positions of leadership for relatively short periods of time. In view of examples of this kind, it seems plausible to argue that where there is no group which is capable of monopolizing the positions of authority, it is virtually impossible for coherent conflict groups to emerge, and the society or association in question is therefore classless. To be sure, this is a kind of classlessness rather different from that of the Utopian anarchy; still, it cannot be denied that it makes sense to speak of classlessness in this case also. We might say that societies and associations governed by a permanently “alternating chairmanship” are classless so far as social mobility is concerned, for it is not the structure of positions but the fluctuation of personnel that in this case prevents the formation of classes and conflict between them.
