5.2 Chapman Democracy Activist Offers a Radical
    Critique of Capitalism
          Interview with Peter McLaren
          J W
P eter McLaren is the coirector o Chapman Uniersity’s Paulo Freire Democratic Project an the uniersity’s international ambassaor or global ethics an social justice. He also happens to be one o the worl’s oremost thinkers on critical peagogy. Critical peagogy is a cross-section between philosophy an eucation that works to equip stuents to ientiy oppression in the worl an eal with it in a constructie manner. McLaren has written an eite nearly ity books in his iel.
    Once an elementary an mile school teacher in Canaa, McLaren
taught at a comortable school in a comortable neighborhoo until one ay he ha a troubling thought: social class is one o the biggest preictors o success, an it was entirely likely that those stuents coul succee not because o their teachers—but, perhaps, een in spite o them. McLaren ecie to go somewhere where he coul hae a more prooun eect—the inner city.
    While others may hae been content with helping at least one o their
stuents, McLaren set higher stanars—he wante to make a ierence or eeryone. He oun a bon with his stuents that his colleagues in’t seem to hae. At one point, he cleare eerything out o his classroom an ille it with rums instea. Rather than teaching the course material, he rumme an connecte with his stuents. Test scores actually went up.
    This was the beginning o McLaren’s path o social justice. Toay, he
encourages critical analysis, especially o capitalism. Inspire by his mentor Paulo Freire, one o the athers o critical peagogy, McLaren stries to equip stuents with the means to make sense o their experiences an look critically at the worl, possibly opening the oor to a better system.

## C    C  l pE dg  g y | 157

    McLaren’s latest book, Pedagogy of Insurrection, was publishe in 2015. In
this book, McLaren raws on his backgroun as a eout Catholic, looking at the teachings o Jesus as they relate to capitalism. In what he expects to be a controersial moe, he argues that Jesus was eectiely preaching communism, an that his message o loe might open the way to a better ethical approach to social justice in moern times.
    We sat own with McLaren to gain some insight on his theoretical work.
Q. Just for starters, how exactly would you describe critical pedagogy? . I think that most people mistake critical peagogy with critical thinking. It’s part o it, but you can’t reuce critical peagogy to critical thinking.
     Critical peagogy has an ethical ounation. You can’t moe rom act to
alue; there’s no logical sequence that will take you rom a critique o capitalism to say that capitalism, thereore, is a ba thing. That requires an ethical jugment. For me, critical peagogy is a philosophy o praxis. Praxis is an interesting term—it’s the ialectical relationship between theory an practice.
Q. What are some of the key ideas you discuss in your books and talks? . Praxis relates to changing the worl. For me, in changing the worl, I name what the problem is. For me, that problem is capitalism—the prouction o alue. Now, alue an wealth are ery ierent. Value is monetize wealth.
    In my work in Latin America an with poor communities all aroun the
worl, I’e come to the realization that critical peagogy requires an ethical commitment. That ethical commitment is to eliminate unnecessary, neeless suering. That’s a moral posture that one takes. Liberation theology calls this a preerential option or the poor. I go arther than that; I say it’s not a preerential option but an obligation.
    Critical peagogy is mobilize towar ining a more humane alternatie to capitalism. Some critics will say that capitalism isn’t perect, but it’s
the best system we hae so we can just try to make it better. It’s the nature o capitalism itsel—the alienation o human labor, the exploitation o human labor through proit making—that makes it impossible to reorm.
    Now, I’m not suggesting that we gie any creibility to Soiet communism or Eastern Bloc police states; they were horrible regimes that misunerstoo, manipulate, an misuse the writings o Marx. So where o we look?
Well, we can look at the Spanish Ciil War, the Paris Commune an we can look at inigenous communities throughout Latin America like the Zapatistas. My emphasis is on encouraging teachers to be public intellectuals—to assume that role, not o being a clerk o the empire. Not to be a unctionary

## 158 | C pE 5

o the school boar but to become transormatie, critical, reolutionary thinkers who take a public stan on the issue o social justice. This notion o proessors an teachers assuming a political neutrality is a alsehoo because i you on’t speak out, that’s take a political position itsel.
Q. Critical pedagogy is meant to instill change, but from where does that change come? . I take a ialectical approach. It’s not simply reolution or reorm; it’s both. Dialectics is about meiation; meiation is not about juxtaposition. It’s not “either or”; it’s “both an.”
    There’s an abstract utopia an a concrete utopia. Abstract utopias are
when someboy has some iea in their min o what a perect worl woul look like, an it’s sometimes ery isconnecte rom what’s actually happening on the groun at the time. Concrete utopias are relate to the problems people are acing in the here an now. You o what you can within the system an hae a larger, broaer ision o where you want to go. Change begins with a critical consciousness but also with a moral an ethical commitment. Join a church group, a community group, a group connecte with public libraries, the uniersity or a high school. Get inole; make a commitment to people an change.
    Critical consciousness isn’t a preconition; it’s an outcome o that engagement. Otherwise, you coul always say you’re not reay. There’s always
another book or philosopher to rea, an it elays you rom taking action. Begin with the action, an use the theory to reine your thoughts about that engagement.
Q. In your latest book, you look critically at capitalism through an interesting lens—the teachings of Jesus. How do you connect these two? . In examining the gospels an statements by Jesus, it became ery clear to me that, while Jesus was not against absolute wealth, he was against relatie wealth or what we call “ierentiating wealth.” That is to say, you cannot be rich while someone else is poor. It became ery clear through his sayings, or what hae been attribute to him, it’s irreutable that a rich person cannot get into heaen. The kingom o Go is really a kingom in which ierentiating wealth oes not exist.
    We coul make an argument that Jesus was teaching communism. Not
the communism that came as a specter, the totalitarian ictatorships we saw with Stalin—I woul not want to een them an woul neer call those regimes “communist.” I mean the notion o sharing resources. The early Christian communities came together to share what they ha. “It’s

## C    C  l pE dg  g y | 159

easier or a camel to pass through the eye o a neele than or a rich man to get into the kingom o Go.” Those statements are not about greey people; they’re statements about society. Toay, how we organize our means o prouction oeretermines the opportunities that some people hae oer others.
    Capitalism structurally instantiates inequality. Jesus equate loe with
the struggle or equality. Loe an justice are the two main messages o Jesus. I you rea the scriptures, Jesus preache a socialist gospel. To see socialism among right-wing eangelical Christians as a kin o eil, it’s absolutely absur. That’s largely a position I think stems out o ignorance about what socialism is an what communism was in the writings o Marx.
    I’m trying to begin a ebate because I eel that ebate is ery important.
I’m beginning to inorm critical peagogy’s ethics o social justice by rawing on the teachings o Jesus.
Q. Historically, many thinkers with dreams of changing the world become disillusioned as they grow older. You’re now approaching your seventies, and your passion is burning brighter than ever. What keeps you going? . I think it’s a certain sense o being blesse. My lie’s been an uphill struggle. Being on this path isn’t easy. I on’t know i you’e rea on the Internet about the “Dirty Thirty,” but I was hit by the Right in 2006. I was labele by a right-wing group with a lot o money behin it as one o the most angerous proessors at UCLA. This group was oering stuents $100 to secretly auiotape my classes an $50 or notes. The only group that stoo up an eene me at the time was the Chaistas in Venezuela. Taking the social justice route can make you a target or right-wing critics who eel you’re against the unamental principles o the country.
     It’s always been a struggle, but I’e elt I’e been blesse in my lie with
the opportunity to meet people rom all oer the worl. I’e oun a loe an a sense o soliarity, a sense o reciprocal relationship base on trust an a share commitment to make the worl a better place. The groups I’e met an the suering I’e seen hae humble me because it makes my suering an personal struggles pale in comparison. I’e met some pretty courageous people that hae inspire me, that hae a commitment an energy that ar excees my own. Meeting my wie, Angie, has been a blessing, an so has haing an opportunity to be at an institution like Chapman, working with stuents an being in a position to learn.
     I’m not optimistic about the uture, but I’m hopeul. I on’t mean hope
in the acile sense; I mean hope as a struggle—as a orm o taking on yoursel the buren o history an knowing that buren is being share by others.

5.3 Neoliberal Globalization and Resistance
    in Education
           e Challenge of Revolutionary Critical Pedagogy
           C Sd
I n this chapter, I escribe the eects o neoliberal globalization on eucation an the response o reolutionary critical peagogy. In my analysis, the terms globalization an neoliberalism are interconnecte an consiere as piotal in a course o commoiication o eucation—namely, the suborination o eucation to the rules o the capitalist market.
     In my iew, the term globalization is nothing more than an ieological
construction that is an attempt to gie a neutral escription o a unamental eature o capitalism that has alreay been escribe by E. Manel in his Late Capitalism—that is, the internationalization o capital an o the exploitatie relations uner the capitalism o multinationals.
     Globalization is accompanie by neoliberal policy, in eect creating
conitions o class war, aiming to increase the proitability o capital at the expense o the oppresse classes ismantling labor relations, halting an re- ersing the gains o the working class worlwie.
     Neoliberal policy is accompanie by an unpreceente ieological oensie at the leel o theory. Typical o this is the use o seemingly neutral terms
that inicate eep reactionary meanings. The aim is the imposition o the ieological hegemony o bourgeois policy an the theoretical isarmament o the exploite classes but most importantly is the eelopment o a consensus system inclusie o the agents o social resistance.
     I beliee it is extremely important to highlight an thoroughly analyze
the neoliberal policy enorcement mechanism in eucation in national eucational systems. This role has been unertaken by the Organisation or Economic Co-operation an Deelopment (OECD) through the Programme or International Stuent Assessment (PISA). An important part o this ar-

ticle iscusses how PISA homogenizes the eucation systems o the OECD country members.
     I consier it important that in aition to the analysis, we shoul also
outline an alternatie proposal, not only in terms o an oerall alternatie economic policy to neoliberalism but particularly at the leel o eucation. Toay, the only coherent proposal against the eects o globalization on eucation comes rom the proponents o reolutionary critical peagogy.
     In this chapter, I outline the prospects o resistance that the anticapitalist/
socialist teachers’ moement inspire by the theoretical accomplishments o reolutionary critical eucation.
NElbEl plCy N EduCN The main objectie o neoliberal policy in eucation is the transormation o eucation to another serices-proiing sector, ultimately a source or increasing the proitability o capital.
     The attempte restructuring o labor relations, o the health an eucation sectors that we witness worlwie, aims to estroy the pillars o the
welare state as expresse by the right to work, eucation, an health an to replace it with a state that is nothing more than a regulator or the proitability o capital at the expense o the weaker social classes.
     Against the neoliberal an neoconseratie restructuring o eucation,
we must propose a ierent policy, to set up a ramework o positions aiming to reerse the neoliberal armageon in eucation. In this irection the answer to the question o the role o eucation uner capitalism has always been o paramount importance.
     The theoretical legacy o classical Marxism guies our position that society shapes eucation in accorance to the nees o the social classes that hol
power. From the stanpoint o the ruling class, the main role o eucation is to reprouce the ominant ieology. Here is part o the iew o eucation as ieological apparatus o the state (ISA). The reprouction o the ominant ieology inoles the istortion o reality, preenting the eelopment o critical knowlege by stuents an the critical unerstaning o the reality o the natural an social worls aroun them.
     But another reprouction process takes place simultaneously. The “eucation inustry” is a signiicant state apparatus in the reprouction an
replication o the capitalist social orm necessary or the continuation o “surplus alue” extraction an economic inequality, so a process o repro- uction o the existing exploitatie social relations through the enhancement o the contraiction between manual an intellectual labor takes place.

## 16 2 | C pE 5

    Nowaays neoliberalism has entere a new stage. The eucational policy
o neoliberalism aims to proletarianize intellectual labor by creating conitions by which eucation will no longer be a springboar or social aancement but a passport or the creation o an army o lexible employees. This is achiee through the ollowing:

       1. the social ealuation o the teaching proession
       2. the interention in the curriculum such that skills come into the
          oregroun an scientiic knowlege (unerstaning by the methos o science) is seconary
In accorance with the argument eelope here, G. Rikowski has suggeste that the capitalist state nees to control the social prouction o labor power or two reasons:

       1. to try to ensure that the social prouction o labor power—equipping stuents with skills an competences—occurs
       2. to try to ensure that moes o peagogy that are antithetical to
          labor power prouction do not and cannot exist. The capitalist state
          will not tolerate any orms o peagogy that attempt to educate

          of themselves as future labor powers and to underpin this awareness
          with critical insight that seeks to undermine the smooth running of
          the social production of labor power
This policy, espite neoliberal maniestations or “reeom o choice,” entails a strict control o the curriculum or teacher eucation, o schooling, an o eucational research.
    My longtime rien an comrae Dae Hill has argue that neoliberal
policy in eucation compresses an represses critical space in eucation, seeking to neutralize an estroy potential pockets o resistance to global corporate expansion an neoliberal capital questioning the role o the school in maintaining an eepening social stratiication, which is what neoliberalism pursues.
Knowledge Society Reaing the arious oicial ocuments o the Worl Bank, the OECD, an the European Union (EU), we see that they start with the premise that we lie in a “knowlege society.” This premise is in act the preamble to their eucation policy proposals.

## C    C  l pE dg  g y | 16 3

    What actually is the “knowlege society”? In essence it is the integration
o knowlege in the prouction process as a irect prouctie orce, as a key actor in the proitability o capital. This new prouction ormat aims to recoer the permanent an structural crisis o the capitalist system, which tries to oercome the crisis through continuous technological reolutions, using knowlege as a irect prouctie orce.
    As mentione beore, the key eature o late capitalism is the proletarianization o intellectual labor. This means that the intellectual worker, such
as a teacher, in the new context, enters the same leel o exploitation as the manual worker. The egree o exploitation o intellectual-mental labor is at the same leel with manual labor. In this phase o capitalism, the egree o exploitation o eucational work is the same as the egree o exploitation o the worker in the actory. This is an essential eature o neoliberal capitalism an the way it is manieste in eucation.
Skills, Not Knowledge The irst thing someone can see in the plans an programs or the “new school” o neoliberalism is the question o eelopment o stuents’ skills. The school curriculum shoul eelop skills such as critical thinking, problem soling, an ecision making. This separation between knowlege an skills an the primacy o skills ersus knowlege was characteristic o the eucational reorm implemente by Margaret Thatcher in Englan in 1979. The irst thing Thatcher i when her goernment esigne the new curricula was to publish an eucational maniesto title “Skills, Not Knowlege.” This slogan constitutes the heart o the neoliberal attack on eucation, an the transormation o the curriculum is centere precisely at this point: the egraation o knowlege an the orientation towar skills in iew o eucating an ajustable lexible worker who will not hae a permanent job an be prepare to change his or her job ie to ten times uring his or her working lie. I so, it oes not make sense to hae concrete, soli knowlege, but the worker shoul eelop speciic skills, so as to be aaptable to changing working enironment uring his or her lie. This package is also associate with lielong learning (LLL). Lielong learning means that eucation nees to gie knowlege that constitutes not the ientity o the knowing subject but speciic skills instea, planning a lexible worker, springy to the working status an lexible in aapting to new working conitions through retraining.
    At the same time, the aim is to inuse in the school curriculum an uni-
ersity eucation terms that may seem neutral but hae a serious ieological loa, to which, unortunately, we hae not gien the importance they esere. That is, there are seemingly neutral terms in eucational policy ocuments

     The OECD policy ollows two main axes: homogenization o eucational
systems an promotion o competition among the participating countries. In the context o this policy, the question ceases to be about eectie eucation, ocusing instea on the position a country achiees in the ranking list o the eucation systems o OECD countries.
     The logic o the OECD strategy supports an increase competitieness
combine with a proceure or establishing common criteria between the eucational systems o countries, as this woul be proitable or the capitalist market. In essence, the mobility in the labor market that shapes globalize capitalism requires homogenize qualiications an skills or the workers o all countries. The aim is to stanarize the eucation systems o OECD countries. To achiee this objectie, PISA ollows the same metho an proceure in all countries employing the same criteria without taking into account the national speciicities o eucational systems an the cultural speciicities o each country. This raises a question o justice, not only or the aliity an reliability o the results but also or the stuents teste. How air is it or stuents to hae to eal with issues o speciic orm an content that may neer hae been taught?
     The questions aske in the PISA test are not base on the objecties an
content o the curricula o the participating countries, because the OECD oes not inten to ealuate the eucation system o each country. “It uses eucational ealuation as a technical homogenization o eucational systems an curricula through competition.”
     Countries participating in the program must align with PISA requirements, because their aim is to achiee the best position in the ranking lists o

eucation systems the OECD publishes. Within this ramework, eucation systems an national curricula are graually homogenize. E pEdggy F ESSNCE The classic book that highlights the position o Marxist critical eucators on globalization in eucation is Peter McLaren an Ramin Farahmanpur’s Teaching against Global Capitalism and the New Imperialism: A Critical Peda-

gogy. The authors’ main goal is to strongly criticize economic globalization, especially its impact: global capitalism, postmoernity, cultural hybriization, an the “new imperialism.” This critique is groune in Marxist theory an as an alternatie orm o eucation oers what is calle revolutionary critical pedagogy.

worl omination, exploitation o labor, support or state terrorism, the militarization o space, the homogenization o meia, an inally a capitalism that conerts the natural enironment into shopping center chains, aiming at short-term proits at the expense o ecological health an human ignity.

     Eentually, howeer, Marxist eucators seem to agree that a global anticapitalist eucation is possible, but to achiee it, we nee to ormulate a new,

pEdggy Some critical eucators question the teacher-centere pedagogy, the pattern o teaching an learning relationships an interaction, what Paulo Freire terme the banking model o eucation. wNESp, CNl, Nd NgEEN F SClS Nd CllEgES Nd uNvESES A ourth question that Marxist an other critical eucators ask is about ownership and control of schools (an, inee, ocational colleges an uniersities). Who shoul own, control, an goern schools an urther eucation at ocational colleges an uniersities? O course we cannot change the law at a stroke, but we can lea a moement so that at some stage—in two years, ten years, twenty years—the ownership an goernance o schools can be change, mae emocratic, secular, an egalitarian, instea o, as in some countries, being owne an goerne by a religious state, transnational corporations, religious organizations themseles, or-proit priate companies, companies that are theoretically not-or-proit but rewar hansomely their executies an their riens, or rich businessmen or women.
     Marxist eucators (an others, o course) beliee that schools, colleges,
an uniersities shoul be run emocratically, with eucators, stuents, an electe representaties o local communities haing powers in an oer those institutions, within a secular, emocratic national ramework. Explicit in this is the assertion that eucation is a public goo an a public right that shoul not be istorte an corrupte by priate ownership—there shoul be no priate schools, colleges, or uniersities.
     A question relate to who shoul own an control schools is that o how
shoul they be manage—to put it cruely, shoul the management style be emocratic, participatie, an collegiate, or shoul it be authoritarian, top-own control? The global moe to priatize state eucation (which has gone a long way in the Unite States an Englan) is a orm o control an management o the workorce—o teachers, lecturers, school support, an aministratie sta—known as new public managerialism—importing the huge ierentials o pay, perks, an power typical o the priate sector into the public sector, into eucation an other public serices such as health an welare an social serices.
xS EduCS What is speciically Marxist about these our questions is that while Marxists work or an willingly embrace reorms such as those that are implicit in the preceing criticisms, we are committe to three orms o analysis an action that social emocrats, raical liberals, raical emocrats, non-Marxist eminists, non-Marxist antiracists, an non-Marxist queer actiists are not.
     What eines Marxists is first our belie that reorms are not sustainable
uner capitalism an that thereore what is neee is a reolution to replace

## 176 | C pE 5

the capitalist economic system with its capitalist economic relations o pro- uction an its capitalist social relations o prouction—the ownership by capitalists o the wealth an the power in society. Reolutions can be iolent (ruling classes o not oten gie up their power peaceully), or they might be through the ballot box or a combination o the two. The ballot box alone cannot bring about reolution because state institutions in capitalism are not emocratic. A Congress or parliament or presient or prime minister has limite power oer these institutions. An electe socialist goernment woul not be able to bring about much change that went against the interests o the capitalist class because the military, juiciary, police, an corporate hierarchy are not emocratic. They use state iolence to stop it. This is the critique, or example, mae by the Greek reolutionary Marxist coalition Antarsya o the raical Let Syriza coalition, which can be characterize as let social emocratic, which is, by current projections (summer 2014), likely to be electe into goernment. But or reolutionary Marxists, a socialist reolution is necessary, so that there comes into power (not just into government) an egalitarian, socialist economic, political, an eucation system.
    The second ierence between Marxist an non-Marxist raicals is that
in orer to replace capitalism, Marxists hae to organize. Thus a Marxist’s uty is to be an actiist, within the limits o his or her ability an competing emans, an to recognize that political organization, program eelopment, an political interention are necessary. Reolutions o not all o trees, like apples. They hae to be ought or an eene.
    The third point is the salience of class as compare with other orms o
structural oppression an iscrimination an inequality. Marxists, Marxist eminists, Marxist antiracists, an Marxist queer theorists stan together with social moements an ciil rights campaigners in opposing racism, sexism, homophobia, an other orms o iscrimination. But Marxists go urther than criticizing (an acting against) social iscrimination an oppression into economic rights—an urther than that, into the recognition that ull economic rights cannot be achiee uner a capitalist economic system but only uner a socialist or communist system.
    Furthermore, Marxists unerstan that only the organize working class
(black-white, male-emale, straight-LGBT, Dalit an all other castes) can organize to replace the capitalist system. These are the points o ierence between Marxists an other raical liberals an letists.
 SClS NFES F EduCN In this section I itemize twenty-one policy aspects an proposals roote in the analysis I hae set out. Many o these are supporte by a ariety o social

## C    C  l pE dg  g y | 17 7

emocratic, liberal, an social justice ieologies, teachers, an citizens. But together they oer a sustaine challenge to neoliberal capitalism. The extent to which they may oer a unamental challenge to capitalism itsel as oppose to the current neoliberal-neoconseratie phase o capitalism (what Trotsky terme a “transitional program”) is a matter or ebate.
    But irst, I wish to acknowlege that to propose eucational theories, we
must always recognize the restrictions place on us uring that process. As Stephen Toulmin puts it, “Until the basic empirical or experimental acts are establishe, we are not in a position to eelop theoretical explanations. Till then the pursuit o theoretical generalizations is premature.” It is ruitul, ollowing such analysis, to engage at a normatie leel o what shoul happen in schools an in an eucation system. This is not inepenent o what oes happen. The normatie section that ollows has been precee by a actual analysis o the history, structure, content, unctioning, an eatures o compulsory state schooling an eucation systems.
    Here I present a program or maniesto or eucation, or iscussion.

    1. Cut class sizes to a maximum of twenty students in both primary and secondary schools. In Englan, or example, many classes in primary (elementary)
schools hae more than thirty pupils—some o the largest in the rich worl— much larger than in priate schools, or example. Accoring to OECD research, Englan is twenty-thir out o thirty eelope countries in terms o large class size. Other countries such as Finlan hae a maximum class size o twenty. Finlan is wiely seen as proiing an extremely high quality o eucation. In countries with a lower GDP, o course, class sizes o ity to a hunre are not uncommon.

    2. Abolish league tables and most externally set assessment tasks. Some external testing is necessary, but the types o regular tests o actual knowlege
typical o the Unite States an Englan (terme “high-stakes testing”) oten restrict teaching to “teaching to the test,” relegates nonactual learning to the sielines, an results in unue stress.

    3. Restore local democratic control of state schools that have been handed over
to private corporations, charities, and individuals to run, and establish local democratic control of such schools. In Englan an Wales such schools are known as academies; in the as the Unite States they are calle charter schools. In a- ition, Englan has state-une free schools run by speciic groups, oten sectional groups o parents. Schools shoul be run by the emocratically electe local councils/municipalities an operate uner national pay an conitions agreements or those who work within the institutions. Rich businesspeople,

## 178 | C pE 5

religious organizations, or “eucorporations”/priate companies running a chain or bran o school shoul not be hane control o state-une schools such as acaemies or charter schools. Also, such prepriatizing pet projects o goernments shoul not be more aantageously une than the rest o the state schools. The ae inestment that goernments put in to prime their aore experiments shoul be kept an enhance, but it shoul be istribute between all schools. Our schools an the chilren in them are not or sale! Schools shoul not, through uneen uning or ierent types o school (e.g., acaemies), be set up or success at the expense o others being set up (an unerune) or relatie ailure.

   4. Form a fully comprehensive secondary school system (known in India as a
common school) so that each school has a broa mix o social class, abilities, an attainment leels.

    5. Get private profiteering out of our schools. Eucation serices that hae
been priatize an hie o an ha their contracts gien to priate proiteers shoul be brought back into public control. These inclue Englan’s Oice or Stanars in Eucation, Chilren’s Serices an Skills (Oste), the boy that runs stuent loans, school meals, cleaning, an caretaking. This is so that proper pay an conitions can be restore to workers whose jobs hae been contracte out, an so that school an uniersity workers who work uner contract can eel that they are part o the school, college, or uni- ersity community. Stuents an school workers who are protesting urther priatization shoul be supporte.

    6. Integrate existing private schools into the state education system so that
the beneits o the priate school system are share among all stuents. All schools shoul be place uner emocratic, locally electe, local council control. Priate schools shoul be prohibite. Religious groups o any religion shoul not be allowe to control an run schools. Say no to big business/ priate capital running our schools an chilren.

     7. “Faith schools” and organized religion should be removed from schools. I
Christians, Jews, Muslims, Hinus, Zoroastrians, Buhists, or members o any religion wish to teach religion, they shoul o it on their own time, at their own places o worship (Saturay/Sunay schools), or in their own supplementary or complementary schools. Ethics, spirituality, an worl religions can be taught, but there shoul be no inoctrination or brainwashing. A critical approach shoul be taken towar religion, recognizing its social an personal unctions but also its political unctions.

    8. Ensure a good, local school for every child. Surplus space means lower
class sizes an increase community use o school acilities.

   9. Make available free, nutritious, balanced school meals for every child to
combat poor iets, obesity, an, or some chilren, actual hunger.

    10. Restore free adult education classes in pastime an leisure stuies as well
as in ocational training/stuies.

     11. Restore or establish free, state-funded residential centers and youth centers/youth clubs or schoolchilren so they can wien their experiences o lie
in sae circumstances an enhance their eucation beyon the conines o the home or city.

     12. Free up curricula that are overprescriptive so there can be more creati-
ity an cross-subject/isciplinary work.

    13. Revise inspectorial and surveillance systems such as (in England) Ofsted. The results o school inspection systems such as Oste in Englan are
to penalize een the best schools (outstaning in eery aspect other than in SAT attainments) in the poorest areas an to strike ear into teachers. Howeer, some external superision an inspection is necessary in extreme cases—or example, to make sure there are no “ghost schools”—that is, to make sure that schools actually exist—an to keep some oersight o school perormance across a range o criteria, a range wier than attainment in tests.

    14. Encourage critical thinking across the curriculum. Teach chilren not
what to think, but how to think. Teach about Marxist analysis an the classexploitatie nature o capitalism. Such critical thinking shoul inclue how to think critically about the meia an politicians an also about Marxist analysis. Critical analysis shoul be sel-critical.

     15. Teach in schools ecological literacy and a readiness to act for environmental justice as well as economic and social justice. Encourage chilren to reach
or the stars an to work or a society that lets that happen—a airer society with more equal chances, pay packets, an power—an teach them about enironmental an sustainability issues.

   16. Ensure that schools are antiracist, antisexist, and antihomophobic—
making sure schools encourage equality an welcome ierent home an group cultures. As part o this, antibullying practices in eery school must be

Bring back the stuy an awareness o the social an political an psychological contexts o teaching, incluing an unerstaning o an commitment to challenge an oerturn racism, sexism, homophobia, an other orms o iscrimination, such as against working-class pupils.

be bought an sol. So there shoul be no ees, as in Scaninaia, Cuba, Venezuela, an Boliia, where eucation up to PhD leel is ree. Say no to uniersity or urther eucation/ocational training ees. An bring in a liing grant or stuents rom less well-o backgrouns.

CNCluSN The “ecay, emoralization an ruin” Trotsky speaks o are or millions o workers—incluing the mile class—an eeryay reality in the current era o capitalism, neoliberal capitalism, or immiseration capitalism. This immiseration is apparent through both rich an poor worls. The organization an characteristics o the resistance to the epreations is a matter or strategic an tactical consierations, relating to the current balance (strength, organization, [is]unity) o class orces in local an national contexts. What is clear, though, is that or Marxist actiists an eucators, it is not enough just to reorm capitalism—welcome though reorms such as the minimum program are—or to actiely campaign or an protect such reorms. Rather, our task is to replace capitalism with emocratic Marxism. As teachers, as eucators, as cultural workers, as actiists, as intellectuals, we hae a role to play. We must play it.

6 | Capitalist Culture and Cultural
         Production
         It will take decades to overcome the inectious, paralyzing inluence o
         bourgeois culture upon the proletariat in the old capitalist countries.
         —Anton Pannekoek, World Revolution and Communist Tactics

         tend to work through political issues, which I think is good, although it’s
         not going to solve the problems.

6.1 The Revolution of Everyday Life
      R V
T he distance between those who possess the most and those who possess
     a small (i ever-increasing) amount has not shrunk; but the intermediate
levels have multiplied and so to speak brought the two extremes, rulers and ruled, closer to the same mediocrity. To be rich nowadays means to possess a large number o impoverished objects.
    Consumer goods tend to lose all use-value. Their nature is to be consumable at all costs, as witness the recent American ad or the “nothing
box”—an object with no conceivable utility. And as General [Dwight David] Eisenhower explained in all candor, the present economic system can be rescued only by turning human beings into consumers, conlating them with the largest possible number o consumable values—which is to say nonvalues, empty, ictitious, abstract values. Ater being “the most precious kind o capital,” in [Jose] Stalin’s happy phrase, human beings must now become the most highly prized o consumer goods. Stereotyped images—movie star, poor person, communist, murderer-out-o-love, law-abiding citizen, rebel, bourgeois—are about to replace humanity with a punch-card system o categories arranged in accordance with an irreutable robotic logic. Already the idea o “teenager” tends to identiy buyers and what they buy, reducing their real variety to a still varied but circumscribed range o commodities (records,
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 187
guitars, Levi’s, etc.). You are no longer as old as you eel or as old as you look but as “old” as what you buy. The time o production, o “time is money,” is giving way to the time o consumption (in both the igurative and the material senses o the word), a time measured in terms o products bought, worn out, and thrown away—the time o that premature old age which is the eternal youth o trees and stones.
     The theory o pauperization is strikingly conirmed today—not, as Marx
expected, in terms o goods necessary or survival, since these, ar rom becoming scarce, have become more and more abundant, but rather in terms o survival itsel, which is ever the enemy o real lie. Modern comorts seemed at irst to promise everyone a lie richer even than the dolce vita o the eudal aristocracy. But in act they turned out to be mere oshoots o capitalist productivity, oshoots doomed to premature old age as soon as the distribution system transormed them into nothing but objects o passive consumption. Working to survive, surviving by consuming and or the sake o consuming: the hellish cycle is complete. According to the logic o the-economyrules, survival is both necessary and suicient. This is the basic reality o the bourgeois era. But it is also true that a historical period based on such an antihuman reality must needs be a period o transition, an intermediate stage between the lie genuinely lived, i less than transparently, by the eudal masters and the lie that will be constructed rationally and passionately by masters without slaves.
     In an economy driven by the production requirements o ree-trade capitalism, wealth alone coners power and honor. As master o the means o
production and o labor power, wealth ensures by extension, thanks to the development o productive orces and o consumer goods, that its owners enjoy a wealth o choice among the ruits o never-ending progress. But to the extent that this orm o capitalism is transormed into an antithetical orm, namely a state-planned economy, the prestige o the capitalist playing the market with his millions ades away, and with it the caricature o the pot-bellied, cigar-puing merchant o human lesh. Today’s managers draw their power rom their organizing skills—even i computer technology already holds them up to ridicule by providing a model that they can never emulate. They are rich in their own right, certainly, but can they vaunt their wealth by having it signiy the potential choices available to them? Can they build a Xanadu, maintain a harem, or cultivate filles-fleurs? Alas, no—or how could money retain its symbolic orce when it is continually solicited and hampered by the imperatives o consumption? Under the dictatorship o consumption money melts away like snow in the sunshine, its signiicance passing to objects with more representational value—more tangible objects better adapted to the welare state and its spectacle. The unction o money

## 18 8 | Ch APr 6

has surely already been sidelined by the market in consumer goods, which, duly wrapped in ideology, have become the true signs o power. Beore long, money’s only remaining justiication will be the quantity o objects and useless gadgets it enables one to acquire and wear out at an ever-accelerating pace; only the quantity and the pace matter, or mass distribution and standardization automatically wipe out quality and rarity. The ability to consume aster and aster, to change your car, your drink, your house, your TV, or your girlriend ever more requently, is now the only index o how much power you can lay claim to in the social hierarchy. From the preeminence o blood to the power o money, rom the preeminence o money to the power o novelties, Christian and socialist civilization has now attained its highest stage: a civilization o the prosaic and the trivial.
     Purchasing power is a license to purchase power. The old proletariat sold
its labor power in order to subsist; what little leisure time remained proletarians spent as best they could in conversation, arguments, tavern games, country matters, going on the tramp, estivity, and riot. The new proletariat sells its labor power in order to consume. When they are not too busy working themselves to death in hopes o a promotion, workers are invited to buy objects—a car, a suit and tie, some culture—that will signal their social rank. We have reached the point where the ideology o consumption becomes the consumption o ideology.
     The system o commercial exchange has now come to govern all o people’s everyday relations with themselves and with their ellows. Every aspect
o public and private lie is dominated by the quantitative.
     The calculation o a human’s capacity to produce or make others produce, to consume or make others consume, is the perect concrete expression
o the idea so dear to the philosophers (and so revealing as to their unction) o the measure of man. Even the simple pleasure o a drive in the country is widely assessed in terms o miles on the clock, speeds reached and petrol consumed. Given the rate at which economic imperatives gobble up eelings, desires, and needs, and pay cash to corrupt them, people will soon be let with nothing but the memory o having once existed. History, in which we shall live retrospectively, will be our sole consolation or our condition o survival. How can real joy exist in a space-time that is measurable and continually measured? Not so much as a hearty laugh. At best, the dull satisaction o the person-who’s-got-his-money’s-worth, and who exists by that standard. Only objects can be measured, which is why all exchange reiies.
     The great ideologies quickly abandon aith or numbers. What is the
nation? Today it amounts to a ew thousand war veterans. And what is what [Karl] Marx and [Friedrich] Engels used to call “our party”? A ew million voters and a ew thousand bill-posters: a mass party.
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 189
     In act ideology’s essence is drawn rom quantity: ideas reproduced again
and again in time (Pavlovian conditioning) and in space (once consumers take up the rerain). Ideology, the news media, and culture—all tend gradually to shed their content and become pure quantity. The less importance a news item has, the more it is repeated, and the more it distracts people rom their real problems. We are a long way rom the Big Lie o which [Joseph] Goebbels said that it was the easiest to swallow. Ideological hyperbole evinces equal conviction to pitch a hundred books, a hundred washing powders, a hundred political ideas, each o which it promotes in turn as ar and away the best. Even in the ideological realm quantity is destroyed by quantity itsel: conditioning is inevitably eroded by its sel-contradictions.
     Could this possibly open an avenue back to power o the qualitative, a
power that can move mountains? Far rom it: sel-contradictory conditioning is prone to produce trauma, inhibition, or a radical reusal to be brainwashed urther. True, ideology can parry this by leaving conditioned individuals choices between lies, by raising spurious questions, alse dilemmas. But such eeble distractions count or precious little in view o the survival sickness to which consumer society exposes its members.
     At any instant boredom can breed an unanswerable rejection o uni-
ormity. Recent events in Stockholm, Amsterdam, and Watts have shown how the merest pretext can precipitate salutary uprisings. What an immense quantity o ot-repeated lies can be swept away by a single burst o revolutionary poetry! From Villa to Lumumba, rom Stockholm to Watts, qualitative agitation—agitation that radicalizes the masses because its source is the radicalism o the masses—eectively pushes back the rontiers o submission and brutishness.
     No one, no matter how alienated, is without (or unaware o) an irreducible core o creativity, a camera obscura sae rom intrusion by lies and
constraints. I ever social organization extends its control to this stronghold o humanity, its dominion will no longer be exercised over anything save robots, or corpses. And, in a sense, that is why consciousness o creative energy increases, paradoxically enough, as a unction o consumer society’s eorts to co-opt it.
     Argus is blind to the danger right in ront o him. Where quantity reigns,
quality has no recognized legal existence; but this is the very thing that saeguards and nourishes it. I have already noted that the dissatisaction bred by the manic pursuit o quantity calls orth a radical desire or the qualitative. The more oppression is justiied in terms o the reedom to consume, the more the malaise arising rom this contradiction exacerbates the thirst or total reedom. The crisis o production-based capitalism pointed up the element o repressed creativity in the energy expended by the worker. The

## 19 0 | Ch APr 6

alienation o creativity through orced labor, thanks to the exploitation o the producers, was denounced once and or all by Marx. Whatever the capitalist system and its successors (even antagonistic ones) have lost on the production ront they try to make up or in the sphere o consumption. The plan is that, as they gradually ree themselves rom their duties as producers, human beings should be trapped by newer obligations as consumers. By opening up the empty lot o leisure time to a creativity liberated at long last by shorter working hours, the well-intentioned apostles o humanism have merely mustered an army it or drilling on the training grounds o the consumer economy. Now that the alienation o the consumer is being laid bare by consumption’s own internal dialectic, one may wonder what kind o prison awaits the highly subversive orces o individual creativity. As I pointed out earlier, the rulers’ last chance here is to turn us all into organizers o our own passivity.
     With touching candor, DeWitt Peters suggests that handing out paints,
brushes, and canvas to everyone who requested them would produce very interesting results. It is true that i this policy were applied in a variety o well-deined and well-policed spheres, such as the theater, the plastic arts, music, writing, etc., and in a general way to any such sphere susceptible o total isolation rom all others, then the system might have a hope o endowing people with the consciousness o the artist, which is to say the consciousness o someone who proesses to exhibit their creativity in the museums and shop windows o culture. The popularity o such a culture would be a clear sign o Power’s success. Fortunately, the chances o people being successully “culturized” in this way are now slight. Do the cyberneticians and their ilk really imagine that people can be talked into “ree experimentation” within bounds laid down by authoritarian decree? Or that prisoners at last aware o their creative capacity might daub their cells with “original graiti” and leave it at that? What would prevent them rom extending their newound penchant or experiment to weapons, desires, dreams, and all manner o means o sel-ulillment? The crowd, ater all, is already ull o agitators. No: the last possible way o co-opting creativity—the organization o artistic passivity—is, happily, doomed to ailure.
     Spontaneity is the mode o being o individual creativity, its original, immaculate orm, neither polluted at the source nor threatened by co-optation.
Whereas creativity is the most equitably distributed thing imaginable, spontaneity seems to be the privileged possession o those whom long resistance to Power has endowed with a consciousness o their own value as individuals. In revolutionary moments this means the majority; at other times, when the revolution must be prepared day by day, it means more people than one might suppose. Wherever the light o creativity continues to shine, spontaneity has a chance.
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 191
     “The new artist protests,” wrote [Tristan] Tzara in 1918. “He no longer
paints . . . but creates directly.” Immediacy is certainly the most succinct, but also the most radical demand that must characterize the new artists o today, who are destined to be constructors o situations to be lived directly. I say succinct because it is important ater all not to be misled by the word spontaneity. The spontaneous can never spring rom internalized constraints, even subconscious ones; nor can it abide alienating abstraction or spectacular co-optation: clearly it is a conquest, not a given. The reconstruction o the individual presupposes the reconstruction o the unconscious (consider the construction o dreams).
     What spontaneous creativity has lacked up to now is the clear consciousness o its own poetry. Common sense has always treated spontaneity as a
primary state, an initial stage in need o theoretical adjustment, o transposition into abstract terms. This view isolates spontaneity, treats it as a thing-initsel—and thus recognizes it only in the travestied orms which it acquires within the spectacle (e.g., action painting). In point o act spontaneous creativity carries the seeds o its eective development within itsel. It is possessed o its own poetry.
     I have already said that creativity, though equitably distributed to all,
inds direct, spontaneous expression only on speciic occasions. These are prerevolutionary moments, the source o the poetry that changes lie and transorms the world.
     Those who make a proession o creating, and those whose proession
prevents them rom creating, both artists and workers, are being pushed into the same nihilism by the process o proletarianization. This process, which is accompanied by resistance to it—resistance, that is, to co-opted orms o creativity—occurs amid such a plethora o cultural goods—records, ilms, paperback books—that once these commodities have been reed rom the laws o consumption they will pass immediately into the service o true creativity. The sabotage o the mechanisms o economic and cultural consumption is epitomized by young people who steal the books in which they expect to ind conirmation o their radicalism.
     What is poetry? It is the organization o creative spontaneity, the deployment o the qualitative in accordance with its coherent inner logic. Poetry is
what the Greeks call poiein, or “making,” but making restored to the purity o its original impulse—restored, in a word, to the totality.
     Without the qualitative, no poetry is possible. The void let by poetry is
illed by poetry’s opposites: inormation, transitional programs, specialization, reormism—all the motley guises o the ragmentary. But the presence o the qualitative does not o itsel ensure the progression o poetry. The richest complex o signs and possibilities may well lapse into conusion, all apart

## 192 | Ch APr 6

or lack o consistency, or crumble by reason o crossed purposes. The yardstick o eectiveness must always remain supreme. Poetry is thus also radical theory completely integrated into action, the mortar binding tactics and revolutionary strategy, and the high point o the great game o everyday lie.
     What is poetry? In 1895, during an ill-advised and seemingly doomed
French rail strike, a militant o the National Railwaymen’s Union stood up and suggested an ingenious and cheap way o advancing the strikers’ cause: “It takes two pennyworth o a particular substance used in the right way to immobilize a locomotive.” It was not very long beore the government and the bosses caved in. Poetry in this case was clearly the act that brought a new reality into being, that reversed the perspective. The materia prima is within everyone’s reach. Poets are those who know how to use it to best eect. Furthermore, two pennyworth o some chemical is as nothing compared with the abundance o peerless ready energy aorded by everyday lie itsel: the energy o the will to live, the power o desire unleashed, the passion o love, the love o passion, the orce o ear and anxiety, the rising tide o hate, and the repercussions o wild destructiveness. Who knows what poetic upheavals may conidently be expected to stem rom such universally experienced eelings as those associated with death, old age, and sickness? This still marginal consciousness will surely be the starting point o the long revolution o everyday lie, the only true poetry made by all and not by one alone.
     “What is poetry?” ask the aesthetes. So, or their beneit, let us state
the obvious: rarely does poetry today involve poems. Most works o art are betrayals o poetry. How could it be otherwise, when poetry and power cannot be reconciled? At best the artist’s creativity builds a prison or itsel, cloistering itsel, awaiting its moment, within an oeuvre that has not yet said its last word; but, however high its author’s hopes, that last word—supposed to herald perect communication—can never be pronounced so long as the revolt o creativity has not yet brought art to its ulillment.
     The Arican work o art—poem, music, sculpture, or mask—is not considered complete until it has become creative speech, an active word: it must
function. This holds true well beyond Arican art. There is no art in the world which does not seek to function; and to unction—later co-optation notwithstanding—in accordance with the will that generated it, the will to live continually in the euphoria o the moment o creation. Why is it that the greatest works never seem to be inished? The answer is that great art cries out in every possible way or ulillment, or the right to enter the world o lived experience. The degeneration o present-day art is a bow perectly readied or such an arrow.
     Nothing can save past culture rom the cult o the past except those
pictures, writings, musical or built architecture, and so on, whose qualitative
                          C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 193
dimension reaches us independently o their orms (contaminated by the decay now aecting all artistic orms).
     As we know, consumer society reduces art to a range o consumable products. This vulgarizing tendency accelerates degeneration but by the same
token improves the prospects o supersession. That communication so urgently sought by the artist is jammed and banned even in the simplest relationships o everyday lie. So true is this that the search or new orms o communication, ar rom being the preserve o painters and poets, is now part o a collective eort. This is the end o the old specialization o art. There are no more artists because everyone is an artist. The work o art o the uture will be the construction o a passionate lie.
     Poetry is always somewhere. I it leaves the realm o the arts, it is all the
easier to see that it belongs irst and oremost in action, in a way o living and in the search or a way o living. Everywhere repressed, this poetry springs up everywhere. Brutally put down, it is reborn in violence. It consecrates riots, embraces rebellions, and animates all great revolutionary carnivals until the bureaucrats place it under house arrest in their hagiographical culture.
     Lived poetry has eectively shown throughout history, even in partial
revolts, even in crime—which [Ernest] Coeurderoy so aptly dubbed the “revolt o one”—that it is the protector par excellence o everything irreducible in mankind, meaning creative spontaneity. The will to unite the individual and the social, not on the basis o illusory community but on that o subjectivity, is what makes the new poetry into a weapon that everyone must learn to handle by themselves. Poetic experience is now at a premium. The organization o spontaneity will be the work o spontaneity itsel.
