6.2 Info-labor/Precarization
       F “B” B
h PYChC CAP f h CmY The digital nervous system incorporates itsel progressively in the organic nervous system, in the circuit o human communication, and recodiies it

## 19 4 | Ch APr 6

according to its operational lines and according to its own speed. But in order to ulill this transormation, the body-mind must pass through an inernal mutation, that we see developing in the history o the world. To understand and to analyze this process, neither the conceptual instruments o political economy nor the instruments o technological analysis are su- icient. The process o production becomes semiotic and the ormation o the digital nervous system co-involves and enervates the mind, the social psyche, desires and hopes, ears and imaginings. Thereore i we want to analyze these productive transormations, we must concern ourselves with semiotic production, with linguistic and cognitive mutations. And mutation passes through the range o pathologies.
     Neoliberal culture has injected into the social brain a constant stimulus
toward competition, and the technical system o the digital network has rendered possible an intensiication o inormatic stimuli, transmitted rom the social brain to individual brains. This acceleration o stimuli is a pathogenic actor that has wide-ranging eects in society. Economic competition and digital intensiication o inormatic stimuli, combined together, induce a state o permanent electrocution that lows into a widespread pathology which maniests itsel either in the panic syndrome or in attention disorders.
     Panic is an ever more widespread syndrome. Until a ew years ago,
psychiatrists hardly recognized this symptom that belonged rather to the romantic literary imagination, and could approach the eeling o being overwhelmed by the ininite richness o the orms o nature by unlimited cosmic power. Today, instead, panic is ever more requently denounced as a painul and worrying symptom, the physical sensation o no longer succeeding in governing one’s own body, an acceleration o the heart rate, a shortness o breath that can lead to ainting and paralysis. Even i to my knowledge exhaustive research does not exist in this area, the hypothesis can be proposed that the mediatization o communication and the consequent rareaction o physical contact can provoke pathologies in the aective and emotional sphere. For the irst time in human history, there is a generation that has learnt more words and heard more stories rom the televisual machine than rom its mother. Attention disturbances are more and more widespread. Millions o North American and European children are treated or a disturbance that maniests itsel as the incapacity to maintain concentrated attention on an object or more than a ew seconds. The constant excitation o the mind on the part o neurostimulant luxes probably leads to a pathological saturation. I we want to understand the contemporary economy we must concern ourselves with the psychopathology o relations. And i we want to understand contemporary psychochemistry we must take into account the act that the mind is invested by semiotic luxes that ollow an extra-semiotic
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 195
principle: the principle o economic competition, the principle o maximum development. From the time when capitalism connected to the brain, the latter incorporated a pathological agent, a psychotic meme that will accelerate pulsations even to tremors, even to collapse.
     In the 1990s, Prozac culture was intermingled with the new economy.
Hundreds o thousands o operators, directors, and managers o the occidental economy took innumerable decisions in a state o chemical euphoria and psychopharmacological lightheadedness. But in the long term the organism collapsed, unable to support indeinitely the chemical euphoria that had sustained competitive enthusiasm and productivist anaticism. Collective attention was supersaturated and this was provoking a collapse o a social and economic kind. As happens in a manic depressive organism, as happens with a patient aected by bipolar disorder, ater the inancial euphoria o the 1990s, there ollowed a depression. It is thereore a case o clinical depression that strikes motivation, initiative, sel-esteem, desire, and sex appeal at the roots. To understand the crisis o the new economy it is necessary to begin rom the psychic experience o the virtual class; it is necessary to relect on the psychic and emotional state o the millions o cognitive workers who animated the scene o business, culture, and the imaginary during the decade o the 1990s. The individual psychic depression o a single cognitive worker is not a consequence o the economic crisis but its cause. It would be simple to consider depression as a consequence o a bad business cycle. Ater having worked or so many years happily and proitably, the value o shares has plummeted and our brainworker is overcome by an ugly depression. It does not happen in this way. Depression descends on the cognitive worker because his or her own emotional, physical, intellectual system cannot indeinitely support the hyperactivity provoked by the market and by pharmaceuticals. As a consequence, things are set to go badly in the market. What is the market? The market is the place in which signs and nascent meanings, desires and projections meet. I we want to speak o demand and supply, we must reason in terms o luxes o desire and semiotic attractors that ormerly had appeal and today have lost it.
     In the Net economy, lexibility has evolved into a orm o ractalization
o work. Fractalization means the modular and recombinant ragmentation o the time o activity. The worker no longer exists as a person. He or she is only an interchangeable producer o microragments o recombinant semiosis that enter into the continuous lux o the Net. Capital no longer pays or the availability o a worker to be exploited or a long period o time; it no longer pays a salary that covers the entire range o economic needs o a person who works. The worker (a machine endowed with a brain that can be used or ragments o time) becomes paid or his or her occasional, temporary services.

## 19 6 | Ch APr 6

Work time is ragmented and cellularized. Cells o time are or sale on the Net and businesses can buy as much as they want without being obligated in any way in the social protection o the worker.
     The intense and prolonged investment o mental and libidinal energies in
the labor process has created the conditions or a psychic collapse that is trans- erred into the economic ield with the recession and the all in demand and into the political ield in the orm o military aggressivity. The use o the word collapse is not as a metaphor but as a clinical description o what is happening in the occidental mind. The word collapse expresses a real and exact pathological phenomenon that invests the psychosocial organism. That which we have seen in the period ollowing the irst signs o economic decline, in the irst months o the new century, is a psychopathic phenomenon o overexcitation, trembling, panic, and inally o a depressive all. The phenomena o economic depression have always contained elements o the crisis o the psychosocial equilibrium, but when at last the process o production has involved the brain in a massive way, psychopathology has become the crucial aspect o economic cycles.
     The available attention time or the workers involved in the inormatic
cycle is constantly being reduced: they are involved in a growing number o mental tasks that occupy every ragment o their attention time. For them there is no longer the time to dedicate to love, to tenderness, to aection. They take Viagra because they don’t have time or sexual preliminaries. They take cocaine to be continuously alert and reactive. They take Prozac to cancel out the awareness o the senselessness that unexpectedly empties their lie o any interest. Cellularization has brought about a type o permanent occupation o living time. The eect is a mutation o social relations in a psychopathic direction. The signs are evident: millions o packets o psychopharmaceuticals sold, an epidemic o attention disturbances spreading among children and adolescents, the becoming normal o the diusion o drugs like Ritalin in schools, and what seems to be the spreading o an epidemic o panic in the abric o everyday lie.
h f-Phr AD h CA mD The mediascape is the universe o transmitters that send to our brain signals according to the most varied ormats. The ino-sphere is the interace between the media system and the mind that receives the signals, the mental ecosphere, that immaterial sphere in which semiotic luxes interact with the reception antennae o the minds scattered on the planet. The mind is the universe o receivers that are not naturally limited to receiving but process, create, and in their turn put in motion new processes o transmission and provoke the continuous evolution o the mediascape.
                       C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 197
     The evolution o the ino-sphere’s activation o always more complex networks o inormation distribution has produced a leap in the power, speed,
and the very ormat o the ino-sphere. There is no corresponding leap in the power and ormat o reception.
     The universe o receivers, human brains o real people made o lesh,
ragile and sensual organs, is not ormatted according to the same standard as the system o digital transmitters. The unctional paradigm o the universe o transmitters does not correspond to the unctional paradigm o the universe o receivers. This asymmetry is maniested by various pathological eects: permanent electrocution, panic, overexcitation, hypermobility, attention disturbances, dyslexia, inormation overload, and saturation o reception circuits.
     At the origin o this saturation, there is a real and proper deormity o
ormats. The ormat o the universe o transmitters has evolved, multiplying its powers, while the ormat o the universe o receivers has not been able to evolve in as rapid a manner, or the simple reason that it is based on an organic support (the human brain-body) that has evolutionary times completely dierent rom the evolutionary times o machines.
     That which is being determined could be deined as a paradigmatic discrepancy, a schism between the paradigm that models the universe o transmitters and the paradigm that models the universe o receivers. In a situation
like this, communication becomes an asymmetrical disturbed process. We could speak in this regard o a discrepancy between cyberspace in unlimited and constant expansion and cybertime. Cyberspace is a network that includes mechanical and organic components whose processing power can be accelerated without limits, while cybertime is an essentially lived reality, linked to an organic support (the human body and brain) whose processing time cannot be accelerated beyond relatively rigid natural limits.
     Since the time when, in 1977, he wrote the book Speed and Politics, Paul
Virilio has maintained that speed is the decisive actor in modern history. It is thanks to speed, Virilio claims, that wars are won, both military and commercial ones. In many o his writings, Virilio shows that the speed o movements, o transportation, o motorization has allowed armies to win wars in the course o the last century. Since then, it has been possible to substitute objects, goods, and people or signs. By virtual, electronically transerable phantasms, the barriers o speed have been broken and the most impressive process o acceleration that human history has ever known has erupted. In a certain sense we can say that space no longer exists, given that inormation can cross it instantly and events can be transerred in real time rom one place to another on the planet, becoming virtually shared events. But what are the consequences o this acceleration on the human mind, on the human

## 19 8 | Ch APr 6

body? To understand it we must make reerence to the capacity o conscious processing, to the capacity or aective assimilation o signs and events on the part o the conscious and sensitive organism.
     The acceleration o inormation exchange has produced and is producing an eect o a pathological type on the individual human mind and even
more on the collective mind. Individuals are not in a position to consciously process the immense and always growing mass o inormation that enters their computers, their cell phones, their television screens, their electronic diaries, and their heads. However, it seems indispensable to ollow, recognize, evaluate, process all this inormation i you want to be eicient, competitive, victorious. The practice o multitasking, the opening o a window o hypertextual attention, the passage rom one context to another or the complex evaluation o processes, tends to deorm the sequential modality o mental processing. According to Christian Marazzi, who has concerned himsel in various books with the relations between economics, language, and aectivity, the latest generation o economic operators is aected by a real and proper orm o dyslexia, incapable o reading a page rom the beginning to the end according to sequential procedures, incapable o maintaining concentrated attention on the same object or a long time. And dyslexia spreads to cognitive and social behaviors, leading to rendering the pursuit o linear strategies nearly impossible.
     Some, like [Thomas H.] Davenport and [John C.] Beck, speak o an attention economy. But when a cognitive aculty enters into and becomes part
o economic discourse, this means that it has become a scarce resource. The necessary time or paying attention to the luxes o inormation to which we are exposed and which must be evaluated in order to be able to make decisions is lacking. The consequence is in ront o our eyes: political and economic decisions no longer respond to a long-term strategic rationality and simply ollow immediate interests. On the other hand, we are always less available or giving our attention to others gratuitously. We no longer have the attention time or love, tenderness, nature, pleasure, and compassion. Our attention is ever more besieged and thereore we assign it only to our careers, to competition, and to economic decisions. And in any case our temporality cannot ollow the insane speed o the hypercomplex digital machine. Human beings tend to become the ruthless executors o decisions taken without attention.
     The universe o transmitters, or cyberspace, now proceeds at a superhuman velocity and becomes untranslatable or the universe o receivers, or
cybertime, that cannot go aster than what is allowed by the physical material rom which our brain is made, the slowness o our body, the need or caresses and aection. Thus opens a pathological gap and mental illness spreads as
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 199
testiied by the statistics and above all our everyday experience. And just as pathology spreads, so too do drugs. The lourishing industry o psychopharmaceuticals beats records every year; the number o packets o Ritalin, Prozac, Zolot, and other psychotropics sold in the pharmacies continually increases, while dissociation, suering, desperation, terror, the desire not to exist, to not have to ight continuously, to disappear grows alongside the will to kill and to kill onesel.
     When, toward the end o the 1970s, an acceleration o the productive and
communicative rhythms in occidental metropolitan centers was imposed, a gigantic epidemic o drug addiction made its appearance. The world was leaving its human epoch to enter the era o machinic post-human acceleration: many sensitive organisms o the human variety began to snort cocaine, a substance that permits the acceleration o the existential rhythm leading to transorming onesel into a machine. Many other sensitive organisms o the human kind injected heroin in their veins, a substance that deactivates the relation with the speed o the surrounding atmosphere. The epidemic o powders during the 1970s and the 1980s produced an existential and cultural devastation with which we still haven’t come to terms. Then illegal drugs were replaced by those legal substances which the pharmaceutical industry in a white coat made available or its victims, and this was the epoch o antidepressants, o euphorics, and o mood regulators.
     Today psychopathy reveals itsel ever more clearly as a social epidemic
and, more precisely, a socio-communicational one. I you want to survive you have to be competitive, and i you want to be competitive you must be connected, receive, and process continuously an immense and growing mass o data. This provokes a constant attentive stress, a reduction o the time available or aectivity. These two tendencies, inseparably linked, provoke an eect o devastation on the individual psyche: depression, panic, anxiety, the sense o solitude and existential misery. But these individual symptoms cannot be indeinitely isolated, as psychopathology has done up until now and as economic power wishes to do. It is not possible to say: “You are exhausted, go and take a vacation at Club Med, take a pill, make a cure, get the hell away rom it all, recover in the psychiatric hospital, kill yoursel.” It is no longer possible, or the simple reason that it is no longer a matter o a small minority o crazies or a marginal amount o depressives. It concerns a growing mass o existential misery that is tending always more to explode in the center o the social system. Besides, it is necessary to consider a decisive act: at the time when capital needed to suck in physical energy rom its exploited and rom its slaves, psychopathology could be relatively marginalized. Your psychic suering didn’t matter much to capital when you only had to insert screws and handle a lathe. You could be as sad as a solitary ly in a bottle, but your

## 200 | Ch APr 6

productivity was hardly aected because your muscles could still unction. Today capital needs mental energies, psychic energies. And these are exactly the capacities that are ucking up. It’s because o this that psychopathology is exploding in the center o the social scene. The economic crisis depends or the most part on a circulation o sadness, depression, panic, and demotivation. The crisis o the new economy was provoked in a large part by a crisis o motivations, by a all o the artiicial euphoria o the 1990s. This has led to eects o disinvestment and in part even to a reduction o consumption. In general, unhappiness unctions as a stimulus to consume: buying is a suspension o anxiety, an antidote to loneliness, but only up to a certain point. Beyond this certain point, suering becomes a demotivating actor or purchasing. There is thereore an elaboration o conlicting strategies. The masters o the world certainly do not want humanity to be able to be happy, because a happy humanity would not let itsel be caught up in productivity, in the discipline over work or in hypermarkets. However, they try out useul techniques to make unhappiness moderate and tolerable, or postponing or preventing a suicidal explosion, or inducing consumption.
     What strategies will the collective organism ollow in order to escape
this abric o unhappiness? Is a strategy o deceleration, o the reduction o complexity possible and able to be hypothesized? I don’t believe so. In human society, potentialities cannot be deinitively canceled out, even when they are revealed to be lethal or the individual and probably even or the species. These potentials become regulated and kept under control or as long as possible, but in the end are inevitably used as happened (and will happen again) with the atomic bomb. A strategy o the upgrading o the human organism is possible—o the mechanical adjustment o the human body and brain to a hyperast ino-sphere. This is the strategy that is used to deine the posthuman. Finally a strategy o subtraction is possible, o distancing rom the vortex, but this is a type o strategy that only small communities can ollow, constituting spheres o existential, economic, and inormatic autonomy with respect to the economic world.
PAC WAr AD m-CAPA Globalization stands reramed in the dark light o the global war. This means we need to reconceptualize the change that is taking place in the social, economic, and anthropological orm o globalization. During the past two centuries, global control was the general techno-utopia o capitalist society and modern culture. Now, the time o global control is over. We are completely out o this ramework today. The new governing ramework o capitalism
                       C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 2 01
is global panic. I we want to understand what panic means we have to talk about the attention economy and about “digital labor.” This is where the source o contemporary panic lies: in the organization o time in the digital sphere, in the relationship between cyberspace and cybertime.
     What is panic? We are told that psychiatrists have recently discovered and
named a new kind o disorder—they call it Panic Syndrome. It seems that it’s something quite recent in the psychological sel-perception o human beings. But what does panic mean?
     Once, panic used to be a nice word, and this is the sense in which the
Swiss-American psychoanalyst James Hillman remembers it in his book on Pan. Pan used to be the god o nature, the god o totality. In Greek mythology, Pan was the symbol o the relationship between man and nature.
     Nature is the overwhelming low o reality, things, and inormation that
we are surrounded by. Modern culture is based on the idea o human domination, o the domestication o nature. So the original panic eeling, which was something good or the ancient world, is becoming increasingly terriying and destructive. Today, panic has become a orm o psychopathology. We can speak o panic when we see a conscious organism (individual or social) being overwhelmed by the speed o processes he/she is involved in, and has no time to process the inormation input. In these cases the organism, all o a sudden, is no more able to process the sheer amount o inormation coming into its cognitive ield or even that which is being generated by the organism itsel.
     Technological transormations have displaced the ocus rom the sphere
o the production o material goods toward the sphere o semiotic goods: the ino-sphere. With this, semio-capital becomes the general orm o the economy. The accelerated creation o surplus value depends on the acceleration o the ino-sphere. The digitalization o the ino-sphere opens the road to this kind o acceleration. Signs are produced and circulated at a growing speed but the human terminal o the system (the embodied mind) is put under growing pressure, and inally it cracks. I think that the current economic crisis has something to do with this imbalance in the ield o semio-production and in the ield o semio-demand. This imbalance in the relationship between the supply o semiotic goods and the socially available time o attention is the core o the economic crisis as well as the core o the intellectual and the political crises that we are living through now.
     We can describe this situation in terms o the relationship between
cyberspace and cybertime. Cyberspace is the ininite productivity o collective intelligence in a networked dimension. The potency o the general intellect is enormously enhanced when a huge number o points enter into

## 2 02 | Ch APr 6

connections with each other thanks to the telematic network. Consequently, ino-production is able to create an ininite supply o mental and intellectual goods. But while cyberspace is conceptually ininite, cybertime is not ininite at all. I call cybertime the ability o the conscious organism to actually process (cyberspatial) inormation. This ability cannot be indeinitely expanded, because it has limits that are physical, emotional, aective. The contradiction between ininite expansion o cyberspace and limited capability o processing o cybertime is the origin o contemporary chaos.
    [Gilles] Deleuze and [Félix] Guattari talk about chaos in What Is Philosophy? They say that chaos occurs when the world goes too ast or your brain.
This is chaos.
    We could recall that Karl Marx had once expressed the concept o an
overproduction crisis. You have an overproduction crisis when machinery and the labor o workers produce an amount o goods that the market cannot absorb. During the history o the industrial system, the overproduction crisis was recurrent, and capitalism was pushed to destroy goods, destroy productive capacity, and also destroy human lives, in order to overcome this kind o economic crisis.
    What is going to happen now? Should we see a relationship between this
big imbalance and the war that is raging and obscuring the horizon o the world? Let’s go back to the concept o panic.
    Semio-capital is in a crisis o overproduction, but the orm o this crisis is
not only economic but also psychopathic. Semio-capital, in act, is not about the production o material goods but about the production o psychic stimulation. The mental environment is saturated by signs that create a sort o continuous excitation, a permanent electrocution, which leads the individual mind as well as the collective mind to a state o collapse.
    The problem o panic is generally connected with the management o
time. But we can also see a spatial side to panic. During the past centuries, the building o the modern urban environment used to be dependent on the rationalist plan o the political city. The economic dictatorship o the last ew decades has accelerated urban expansion. The interaction between cyberspatial sprawl and urban physical environment has destroyed the rationalist organization o space.
    In the intersection o inormation and urban space we see the prolieration o a chaotic sprawl ollowing no rule, no plan, dictated by the sole logic
o economic interest. Urban panic is caused by the perception o this sprawl and this prolieration o metropolitan experience; the prolieration o spatial lines o light. The metropolis is a surace o complexity in the territorial domain. The social organism is unable to process the overwhelmingly complex
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 2 03
experience o metropolitan chaos. The prolieration o lines o communication has created a new kind o chaotic perception.
    In their book The Attention Economy, Davenport and Beck say that the
central problem o the cognitive worker, and generally o people who are living in hypersaturated inormational environments, is this: we have no more time or attention, we are no more able to understand and process inormation input because our time is saturated by a low o hyperinormation. We don’t have time or attention in the workplace. We are orced to process ar too large amounts o inormation and our body-mind is completely taken by this. And urther, we have no time or aection, or communication, or erotic relationships. We have no more time or that spatial kind o attention that means attention to the body—to our body, to the body o the other. So, more and more, we eel that we have run out o time; that we must accelerate. And we eel simultaneously that acceleration leads to a loss o lie, o pleasure and o understanding.
CmP The concept o competition has replaced that o competence.
     Competence is the intellectual skill that enabled the bourgeoisie to carry
out its planning, administrative, and organizational unction, and justiied its right to property.
     Ever since the technologies o intelligence made it possible to standardize the processes o planning, coordination, and administration that once
coalesced with the role o ownership, intellectual unctions have turned into the unctions o subordinate labor.
     The competent bourgeoisie was replaced by a class that turned competition
into the only rule and competence. However, when speaking o competition, is it not obvious that the most competitive is the one who can eliminate the adversaries? And when it comes to eliminating adversaries things get serious.
     As property came to coincide with a dusty cloud o ractions o investment rather than with the person, competition replaced competence. Many
competences are still necessary to production, but they are now detached rom the role o the enterprise. Any intellectual competence that is not related to speculation is made precarious, devalued, and low waged.
     Crime is no longer a marginal unction o the capitalist system but the
decisive winning actor or deregulated competition. Torture, homicide, child exploitation, the drive to prostitution, and the production o instruments o mass destruction have become irreplaceable techniques o economic competition. Crime is best suited to the principle o competition.

NOTE This piece is excerpted rom Franco “Bio” Berardi, Precarious Rhapsody: Semiocapitalism and the Pathologies of the Post-Alpha Generation (London: Minor Compositions, 2009). Reprinted by permission o Minor Compositions (http://www.minorcompositions.ino/).

6.3 Imaginal Machines
          S S
T here has long existed both an intense and troubled connection between
     avant-garde arts and autonomous politics. That a composition is aesthetically innovative does not necessarily mean that it’s particularly progressive
politically, either in its content or the relations animated through its creation and circulation. The Futurists do not just represent the point where, as Stewart Home explores, utopian currents based around the integration o all aspects o lie, which were viewed primarily as religious during medieval times, moved over to be considered part o the artistic sphere. The Futurists are perhaps the clearest indication that interesting aesthetics do not necessarily correspond to progressive politics, as their intense ascination with war, speed, patriotism, and eventual movement toward ascism display. There are countless more examples less dramatic but all the more complex, ranging rom surrealism’s turbulent relation to the institutional communist movement (and eventual severing and usage by U.S. state propaganda eorts and ronts) to debates about the role o street art in gentriication.
     One recurring challenge or political art is to circumvent the assumption,
implicitly contained within a didactic composition, that the work’s arguments can take place in an already existing public sphere—the common ground and rame o reerence—that preexists the particular expression. Unaware o this challenge, much political artwork strives to create interesting and compelling arguments, lourishes o speech, in hopes that the message will reach the listener with little intererence. In order or political speech to cause aective resonance, conditions need to exist or the constituted audience to be able to identiy with those who are expressing them, to possess a capacity to aect and be aected. This process o aective composition so oten begins rom minor moments and interactions: yet through them spaces o commonality, where new relations and interactions are possible, emerge. [Gilles] Deleuze and [Félix] Guattari’s observation that “the people are missing” is not a lament but a realization that the task o politics is precisely the composition o common space through processes o intensive engagement not bound by the closure o already understood identities and positions. This is precisely the point explored by Randy Martin in his work on the role
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 2 05
o theater in the producing and orming o publics, acting as a means or the production o socialist ensembles: “publics must continue to be generated i a revolutionary project is to maintain its resonance.” For Martin autonomy is not something granted but rather a critical presence existing through and despite networks o governance. The continual generation o new publics, o new orms o the resonance o ideas and relations, is the process o aective composition, whether through the orming o publics through theater or any other o the possible means.
     But what is meant by aective composition? At the risk o launching yet
another trendy neologism, the concept o aective composition is ormed by the bringing together o notions o aect with the autonomist notion o class composition. The concept o aect was developed in a submerged history o philosophy, stretching rom Spinoza to Deleuze and Guattari (and having been developed urther by igures such as Antonio Negri and Genevieve Lloyd), to indicate an increase in capacity to aect or to be aected by the world. For Deleuze and Guattari, artistic creation is the domain o aective resonance, where imagination shits through the interacting bodies. Composition is used here, borrowing rom the autonomist Marxist notion o class composition, indicating the autonomous and collective capacities to change the world created through social resistance. As orms o collective capacity and sel-organization are increased, composed by the circulation o struggles and ideas, the workings o the state and capitalism attempt to ind ways to disperse them or to integrate these social energies into their own workings. Thus there are ormed cycles o the composition, decomposition, and recomposition o struggles. A key insight o autonomist thought was the argument that the nature o struggles and the orms o social cooperation created within in them determine the direction o capitalist development, rather than the autonomous sel-directed power o capital. Considering aective composition through orms o street art and perormance is to look at the ways that the capacities they create contribute to the development o aective capacities and orms o sel-organization. It is the ways in which street arts can take place in what the Inernal Noise Brigade mission statement describes as “acilit[ating] the sel-actualization o the mob.” This sel-actualization is not something which ever reaches a inal or inished point, but continually doubles over and immanently above itsel, turning cycles o struggles into spirals o movement composition.
     The aective composition o relations and intensities in aesthetic politics
is a pressing question because o ways that the possibilities or the existence o public and common space have changed over recent years. The increasingly drastic commercialization o public space, corporate domination o media outlets, and predominance o earmongering in all areas o lie has created a

## 206 | Ch APr 6

condition where there are immense lows o inormation and data available or discussion but precious little public sphere in which this data can resonate. Paolo Virno argues that where orms o collective intelligence do not ind expression in a public sphere where common aairs can be attended to, it produces terriying eects and prolierations o unchecked and groundless hierarchies. These are areas o “publicness without a public sphere.” There are lows o inormation and images constantly surrounding and immersing us that allow or new possibilities or communication and the ormation o subjectivities, but which can also be quite overwhelming and go in directions that are not necessarily liberatory. Chat rooms and blogs meld seamlessly with the commercial landscapes o gentriied cities and the twenty-ourhour-a-day low o “news” that may excite the libido or intone the constant reminder o “be araid” but do not constitute a common place o collective engagement. More than anything they tend to proactively prevent the emergence o shared space in ways that have not been overcoded by the workings o state or capital.
     One cannot assume that there is an already existing public sphere, an existing arrangements o bodies, ready to receive inormation conveyed through
an artistic composition. Relying on the expected aesthetics o propaganda means circumscribing possible patterns o resonance more limited than might be wished. Political art derives its politics not just by its content but also by the ways in which it is designed to work with or against the predetermined orms o circulation o ideas, images, and relations. In other words, to appreciate that orms o street art do not derive their subversiveness simply rom the act that they occur on the street (which can also include a whole range o viral marketing and quotidian orms o spectacular recuperation), but rather rom unolding the relations that avoid the overcoding operations o the art institution and commodity production. It is this ocus on patterns o circulation and relations as a politico-aesthetic activity, what George Katsiaicas describes as “engaging aesthetic rationality in the process o political transormation, o turning politics into art, everyday lie into an aesthetically governed domain,” that comprises the process o aective composition.
     One approach to understanding relationships and the construction o
community in an artistic ramework recently has been Nicolas Bourriaud’s notion o “relational aesthetics,” which he developed during the mid-1990s coming out o his curatorial work. Relational aesthetics, which Bourriaud rames as part o a materialist tradition, is argued to represent a theory not o art but o orm—namely, one where “intersubjectivity does not only represent the social setting or the reception o art . . . but also becomes the quintessence o artistic practice.” Collective understandings, experiences, and interactions then are not something that is added on the work aterward
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 2 07
but rather compose the starting point and substance o the work itsel. While any artwork can be thought o as a relational object through the kinds o interactions it animates (otentimes a gentle stare with a determined air o trying to appear clever), the dierence here is that these relations are the core o the work itsel. Bourriaud argues that today the designation o art seems to be little more than a “semantic letover” which should be replaced by a deinition like “art is an activity consisting in producing relationships with the world with the help o signs, orms, actions, and objects.”
     From this Bourriaud tries to recast the critical unction o aesthetic intervention, arguing that rather than being based on orming imaginary and
utopian realities, artistic intervention is aimed at orming living models o action and being within the existing world. The artistic composition exists then as “a social interstice within these experiments and these new ‘lie possibilities’ appear as possible.” A piece’s aura no longer lies in another projected world, or in the orm itsel, but rather in the temporary collectives and communities that coalesce or the purposes o producing the exhibition or the space itsel (although or Bourriaud clearly this is all about the exhibition rather than any diuse process o creation going beyond the gallery walls). For Bourriaud the subversive and critical unction o artistic creation is the invention o individual and collective vanishing lines, lines o light, in the creation o temporary nomadic constructions.
     At irst this seems reasonable enough, and relatively close to what I’m
describing as the process o aective composition, the movement rom artistic creation based around objects to the creation o relations and modulations o aect. But slowly the situation becomes more problematic. For instance, there’s no necessary dichotomy between orming imaginary and utopian realities and micropractices o intervention. Utopian dreams and models, rather than being stated and ixed models to impose, have oten acted as inspirations or inding micropolitical modes o intervention, which is to say that they have oten been connected rather than mutually exclusive. Likewise one can build an overall vision drawing rom and extending micropractices. But what might at irst appear to be a relatively minor dierence not worth quibbling about that much becomes more pronounced, especially when Bourriaud makes the argument that today “social utopias and revolutionary hopes have given way to everyday micro-utopias and imitative strategies, [and] any stance that is directly ‘critical’ o society is utile.” Rather than there being just a distinction between imaginary and utopian projects, here it is argued that these projects insoar as they are directly critical o the social world (the state, capitalism, war, exploitation) are utile. In other words, dear artist, give up any hopes o having anything to say about social conditions on any large scale and make yoursel content with micro-interventions and practices.

## 208 | Ch APr 6

This sounds quite similar to approaches ascribed to politics and thought coming in the wake o 1968 and poststructuralism and so-called identity politics. In other words, a shit in politics, rom grand to minor, in a way that micropractices and interventions are divorced rom large questions and social structures.
     This does not mean that all practices and questions have to be directly
oriented to addressing large-scale social and historical projects, as might be argued by the looming clichéd image o the overly zealous Trotskyite who insists that everything must be about directly contesting capitalism rom the perspective o the working class or it is “objectively reactionary.” There are many ways to congeal and conjoin minor articulations inormed by and relating to large social questions that are undamentally concerned with an overall social critique, without having that critique determine and overly con- ine these practices.
     But let us return or a second to a particular kind o moment o the breaking down o barriers through aective composition, namely through orming
an aective space through and around the perormance o radical marching bands. A moment where the passivity o the crowd perhaps is broken and the nature o the space is transormed. Bodies milling about, held awkwardly at a distance, a space maintained and looks a little chilly. Not rom malice or mistrust but rom not knowing. But in that instant borders all. The irst hit o the drum is the irst crack in the wall o the objectiying, separating gaze, the space created by the passive stare o an audience toward a perormance, an exhibition: a spectacle. As the melody pulses through the crowd we revel in the timbre o the horns. Arms, words, memory, and noise tenuously connect through time and desire. Ideas, memories, histories, cultures, and stories are crossbred. Rage blends with joy; dislocation is replaced by emerging, momentary worlds.
     It is in this sense that radical marching bands are o the most interest: in
the ways they undercut the usual space (and sometimes relations) o perormance and create mobile and aective spaces in the streets where it becomes possible or other orms o relations to emerge. Projects such as the Hungry March Band, the Inernal Noise Brigade, and Rhythms o Resistance, closely connected with the late 1990s upswing in streets protests and parties such as Reclaim the Streets, brought carnivalesque energies and excitement into the all too oten ritualistic and stale mode o political protest. One could argue, perhaps paradoxically, that while in the upswing o organizing and summit protests that occurred in the post-’99 realization there already were existing movements against the more egregious excesses o the state and capitalism; there was somewhat o a shit away rom diuse orms o cultural politics to more spectacular media-riendly orms such as mass mobilizations and
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 2 09
lockdowns. Radical marching bands and other orms o tactical rivolity were important in keeping open space or the emergence o intensive and aective relations within such spaces, relations which hopeully would ind their ways out on to all o the abric o daily lie.
     Hakim Bey describes how marching bands were invented by Turkish
Janissaries, members o the Ottoman Imperial Guard, who belonged to a heterodox Bektashi Sui order. The marching band, developed or use in military campaigns, unctioned as a orm o psychological warare through music that induced sheer terror in their opponents. Their eect on European armies, who had never experienced anything like that previously, must have been complete ear, most likely resulting in increased morale among the Turkish troops. Marching bands were adopted by European states or use in military campaigns and increasingly in symbolic and ceremonial unctions as orms o ampliied communications technologies became developed. Forms o marching band music moved with the migration o Roma people rom the Ottoman Empire to the southern United States, who brought along with them brass instruments that had a proound eect on the development o music in the area.
     Not surprisingly, then, the repertoire o many marching bands is also a
veritable melting pot o styles, cultures, and background, bringing together anything rom jazz and big band tunes to klezmer, Moroccan music and Indian wedding tunes to calypso, salsa, reggae, and Sun Ra. There are also large degrees o inspiration rom projects that have merged together the energy o punk rock and street perormance, such as Crash Worship and ¡TchKung! (who had members that went on to orm marching bands). There are large degrees o crossover and mixing between political marching bands and other orms o street and perormance art and theater (such as Vermont’s Bread & Puppet Theater, which provided a key source o inspiration or many marching bands) as well as underground circus and vaudeville (such as the Bindlesti Family Cirkus and Circus Contraption).
     One o the best examples I can think o or how a marching band altered
the composition o a situation occurred at the Foo Festival in Providence, Rhode Island, in July 2006. The event, organized by people rom AS220, a local arts space, illed the greater part o a city block while literally thousands o people milled about attending various talks and workshops, casually munching on ood, browsing through the wares o booksellers, and watching bands and musicians perorm on a stage located near one end o the estival. At several points during the day the What Cheer? Brigade, a local marching band, would materialize replete with propulsive drumming and piercing horns, resplendent in motley attire that one would be hard pressed to call uniorms. Their appearance changed the nature o the situation because as

## 210 | Ch APr 6

they would enter the space people would begin to dance and rolic around with them as they moved through the space, rather than staying ixed on the stage as a ocal point, one that clearly marked the dierence between those who were perorming and observing. This increase in the generalized level o conviviality aected not only those directly involved in the dancing but seemed to move beyond itsel as those around it somehow ound new reasons to converse and interact with people they hadn’t spoken with beore.
    The marching band may most commonly be experienced as an appendage to the state orm, as a space deined by tightly scripted and controlled
lines and the military insignia. They are encountered at the military or civic parade, or perhaps as a motivational soundtrack to a sports competition. And it is perhaps this association that makes their playul détournement and reappropriation to serve other ends all the more delicious. March music might usually typically have resonance with the workings o the war machine, but as Deleuze and Guattari would remind us, this war machine can never totally be integrated into the workings o governance: there is always something that escapes. It is a process that exceeds that subject and existing communicative structures yet paradoxically one that creates a space where the possibility o transversal commonality exists. And the war machine, understood as a space o exteriority to the state, can also be understood as a trans- ormation machine, as the nomadic lows and machinations that constitute spaces o possibility.
    Stencil art and graiti as well as street perormances play an important
role in breaking down the orms o relations created by artistic activity as separated or removed rom daily lie because they can be inscribed within the lows o people’s everyday lives, in the streets, and in subways. But this does not inherently mean that such activities contain the possibility or reorienting people’s expectations or will result in certain responses. And indeed, it is possible that what was once an innovative creative activity can become standardized and expected in such ways that the aectivity it initially generated is longer as intensive or eective in its workings. And i Banksy, or someone as marketable as him, should come to your town, it can drive up the real estate values as well. Even the most apparently subversive imagery can be reincorporated and recuperated back into the workings o the spectacle.
    This constituent and aective space or creating new relations is not one
that can be created and continue to exist without intererence or diiculty. Temporary autonomous zones are temporary or a reason—namely, the realization that attempts to create such spaces will inevitably ace repression and recuperation. Thus, it is oten not tactically sensible to create a space and maintain it (investing time, energies, and cost) against all odds. These moments and spaces are described quite well by the Leeds May Day Group
                        C A P A     C u  u r  A  D C u  u r A  PrDuC   | 211
as “moments o excess.” One can see how with phenomena as diverse as the rise o punk and social centers to culture jamming and Critical Mass, through dierent mixes o co-optation and legal action, a space that once vibrant and ull o possibilities comes to be a bit lackluster. But the compositional capacities o these ruptures are not unlimited, or they too through repetition become ritualized and all back into solidiied patterns o circulation. The question becomes one o keeping open the aective capacities o the created space: to ind ways to avoid the traps o spectacular recuperation and the solidiication o constituent moments and possibilities into ixed and constituted orms that have lost their vitality.
     It’s in this way that the concept o the art strike, as originally proposed
by Gustav Metzger, and then urther developed by Stewart Home and the Praxis Group in 1990–1993, becomes useul or the composition o struggles. At a juncture where capitalism is increasingly reliant on the production o new images, relations, and aects or its continued existence, [it] struggles to ind ways to intervene in these somewhat more ephemeral realms. This is why Home argues that the importance o the art strike lies less in its easibility or its concrete success, but rather in the possibility it opens up or extending and intensiying class war: “By extending and redeining traditional conceptions o the strike, the organization o the art strike intends to both increase its value both as a weapon o struggle and a means o disseminating proletarian propaganda.” The withdrawal o artistic labor needs to be collective in a signiicant way to have any eect, or to only have one artist striking against the institutional machinery (as with Metzger’s irst strike), or a handul (as with the Praxis Group), while quite conceptually interesting, has little in the way o eects. The withdrawal o artistic labor can only be aided by disruption o artistic production and communication by inducing o conusion and distortion o communication guerillas in the mystiications o the post-Fordist world. As the autonome a..r.i.k.a. gruppe argues, “When inormation becomes a commodity and Cultural Capital a most important asset, the distortion and devaluation o both is a direct attack against the capitalist system. To say it in a swanky way: This is Class War.” This is much the tactic explored in the discussion o the IWW Starbucks campaign: insoar as the production and ambiences is central to Starbucks, monkey wrenching their image production process can be understood as industrial action. Or, as Patrick Reinsborough describes it, “direct action at the point o assumption.”
     This would mean to work with a sense o aesthetic politics and interventions that are not necessarily or totally based on the elements contained
within the work itsel, but on understanding the possibilities created or aective relations, spaces, and interactions and their intensiication and

## 212 | Ch APr 6

deepening by the process o artistic creation. And to continually modiy the composition o these situations, subtracting them rom circulation and visibility, and turning toward disruption, conusion and illegibility, in the ace o recuperation. This is to understand artistic creation as what George Kubler describes as the shaping o time: art as a succession o works and productions distributed through time that embody the development o orms o collective time and relations. That is, a process that is not necessarily predicated on the creation o meaning, but as an intervention or opening into a system o relations, connecting innovations that are passed along and mutated through the modulation o the relations in which they exist, on a terrain and topology o time “where relationships rather than magnitudes are the subject o study.” It is in these chains o relations that radical innovations in work, orm, idea, and practice are passed along, mutated, and linked in a succession o works embodying orms o collective time and relations.
